The clock hit 21:47.

On camera two, the site manager stepped into the egress corridor.

The window closed. It closed the way mathematical windows close: quietly, absolutely, and without the possibility of negotiation.

"Primary route is dead," Bell said. She disconnected the hardline tap and packed the terminal into her rig. "Transition to the third vector. Maintenance corridor. West panel override."

"Copy. Moving."

Bell descended the gantry ladder. The third vector required convergence at the west panel — Hayden arriving from the server farm, Bell arriving from the gantry. Her transit time: forty-two seconds.

She hit the ground floor at a sprint, measuring her footfalls against the concrete to minimize the acoustic spike. The temperature dropped as she moved deeper into the maintenance architecture.

The ozone arrived at second twenty-eight.

Heavy. A chemical saturation that flooded the corridor, thick enough to taste — the violent, electrical sharpness of a relay tower operating at peak capacity. It stripped the diesel and the cold concrete entirely from the air.

She rounded the final corner at second forty.

Hayden stood at the west panel. He had the facilities key in his right hand, the steel housing of the manual override in front of him.

"Panel secured," he said. He reached for the lock.

Bell closed the remaining three metres when the physics of her partner broke.

The corridor ran under industrial halogens, absolute and unforgiving. No fluorescent fatigue. No shadow to blame.

Hayden pushed the key toward the slot. His hand — the right hand, the one he favoured, the one that had held the green mug and annotated the floor plan — met the steel faceplate of the lock housing.

It did not stop.

The hand passed through the solid steel. The flesh lost its density, turned a translucent, bruised grey, occupying the same spatial coordinates as the metal. The key — inorganic, unbound to Hayden's biology, still governed by baseline physics — struck the solid faceplate, slipped from his incorporeal fingers, and hit the concrete floor with a sharp, echoing clatter.

Hayden froze. His hand remained buried to the wrist in the steel panel.

Ozone pressed against Bell's sinuses like a physical weight.

Her cognitive architecture — the system of categories and probabilities she used to process the world — attempted to file the event. *Visual anomaly. Lighting distortion. Fatigue.* The filing mechanism jammed. The data refused the categories. The categories shattered.

For two seconds, neither of them breathed.

Then the hand snapped back to opacity. Solid. Present.

Hayden pulled his hand free. The movement came sharp, jerky — the first uncalculated motion she had witnessed from him in four years. His gaze dropped to his hand, then to the key on the floor.

He did not reach for it with his right hand.

He knelt, retrieved the key with his left, and inserted it into the west panel lock. His left hand shook — a gross motor tremor, the kind that belonged to adrenaline dumps and neurological failure. He turned the key.

The panel opened. He hit the manual override.

Twelve metres away, the loading bay roller door engaged its twelve-second hydraulic cycle.

"Move," Hayden said.

He bypassed her entirely, heading for the egress, his right hand pulled tight against his chest in a protective posture that belonged to a different, older set of human instincts.

Bell followed.

They cleared the door at second eleven. They crossed the Footscray Road tram corridor in the dark, moving at operational pace, two figures visible from two hundred metres, relying on the twelve-minute service gap the math promised.

The math held. They reached the van.

Hayden got in the passenger side. Bell took the wheel.

She put the vehicle in gear and merged onto the arterial road, holding her speed at exactly two kilometres below the posted limit to avoid algorithmic traffic flagging. The van smelled of stale upholstery and, faintly, of ozone.

Hayden sat in the passenger seat. He stared straight ahead. His right hand lay flat on his thigh, perfectly solid, perfectly still.

Bell drove.

She opened the mental file labeled *visual anomaly*. She stripped the label. She deleted the rationalizations — the fluorescent tubes, the fourteen-hour days. She examined the raw data: her partner had experienced a localized, transient failure of physical matter. He knew about it. He had been hiding it.

She did not ask him what had happened. Operational planners do not ask questions when they already possess the data required to form the conclusion.

The category reassigned itself: *Critical systemic failure. Uncontained.*

The primary route had collapsed at exactly 21:47. The contingency had worked. They were out. But as Bell drove them back toward the Fitzroy safe house, navigating the flat, functional geometry of the city at night, she understood that the architecture of their partnership had just suffered the same kind of failure as Hayden's hand — structural, silent, and past the point of reversal.



<a name="chapter-6"></a> [home](#home)

# CHAPTER 6 — ALFRED HOSPITAL

Reality B-734 smelled of copper and standing water.

That was the first thing — always the first thing, coming through. Baseline smelled of nothing: recycled air, human use, anonymity. B-734 smelled of mineral-sharp water sitting in old pipes and copper oxidation in relay infrastructure the QSA had never upgraded because B-734 was a monitoring node, not an operational one, and monitoring nodes received maintenance on a seventeen-year cycle.

Hayden had been running this insertion point for eight months.

He stood in the maintenance corridor behind the hospital's eastern substation and waited for his legs to work.

This was the second thing — always the second thing. The legs. Transition from B-734 to baseline required the body to recalibrate its proprioceptive model from scratch. The gravitational constant in B-734 ran 0.3 percent heavier than baseline, and the body, after even a short stay, adjusted its muscle tension to match. Coming back meant the legs expected more resistance than the floor provided. For approximately ninety seconds after transition, the body moved through baseline as though the ground might give way.

Four hundred and twelve transitions. Ninety seconds of unreliable legs, four hundred and twelve times.

He pressed his back against the corridor wall and counted his pulse. Not at the wrist — the radial pulse had gone unreliable six months ago, the vessel walls thickening in a pattern Winters had described in her notes as *coherence calcification*: the body's connective tissue attempting to stabilize itself against repeated quantum state disruptions by depositing calcium in the arterial walls. He counted at the carotid instead. Ninety-one. High, but within post-transition range. It would drop to seventy-eight within four minutes.

The ICS reading on his subdural monitor pulsed once — a single blue flash behind his left eye, the notification he'd configured to replace the audio alert he'd disabled eight months ago when the audio alert began triggering the auditory fragmentation.

The reading: *34*.

Thirty-four. Down from thirty-seven at his last check, six days ago.

The Identity Coherence Score measured the degree to which the body's quantum state remained internally consistent — the degree to which the cellular-level information that made a specific person a specific person held together across repeated transitions. A baseline human, never transitioned, scored between 95 and 100. The QSA's operational threshold for active field personnel was 70. Below 60, the agency's medical protocols required mandatory stand-down and coherence stabilization treatment.

Below 40, the protocols had a different name. Hayden had read them once, in a file he wasn't supposed to have access to, and had not read them again.

Thirty-four.

His legs steadied. He pushed off the wall.

---

The hospital's eastern service entrance used a standard QSA-adjacent security protocol — the same biometric lock architecture the agency had licensed to civilian medical facilities under the 2149 Public Infrastructure (Quantum Security) Agreement. Hayden had the override codes. He had obtained them from the same source as everything else: a QSA internal archive he'd accessed during his third month of operation, when his ICS had still been at 61 and his hands had still been reliable and the transition headaches had lasted four hours instead of eleven.

The lock opened. He went in.

---

The service corridor ran the length of the eastern wing's ground floor, parallel to the public corridor but separated by a wall of reinforced concrete the hospital's 2134 renovation had installed to meet updated seismic standards. The lighting was motion-activated and came on in sections as he moved — a thirty-metre pool of fluorescent white, then darkness, then another pool as the next sensor triggered.

Jacob was at the junction.

He was doing his job. Standing at the intersection of the service corridor and the stairwell access, his back to the wall, his attention on the tablet in his left hand — the shift log, updated every thirty minutes, the kind of task that occupied the hands without occupying the mind. A large man, broad through the shoulders, carrying the physical confidence of someone who had spent years in a role that occasionally required it. His ID badge hung from a lanyard at chest height.

He looked up when the motion sensor triggered the next pool of light.

Hayden was already inside the sensor's range. He kept walking. His pace did not change.

Jacob's hand moved toward the radio at his hip. Standard protocol — unscheduled personnel in a restricted corridor, radio first, questions second.

Hayden closed the distance in four steps. His right hand found the point at the junction of Jacob's neck and shoulder — the brachial plexus, the nerve cluster that, under sufficient targeted pressure, produced immediate motor disruption in the arm and a pain response that overrode every other cognitive process for approximately two seconds. Two seconds was enough. His left hand controlled the radio before it cleared the holster. He took Jacob's weight as the man's legs went, guided him to the floor with the practiced efficiency of someone who had done this before and had learned, through repetition, that the difference between a controlled descent and an uncontrolled one was the difference between a guard who woke up with a headache and a guard who didn't wake up at all.

Jacob's head touched the floor. His breathing held — steady, unconscious, the slow rhythm of someone who had been removed from the situation rather than damaged by it.

Hayden took the radio. Took the tablet. Checked the shift log for the next scheduled check-in: forty-two minutes.

He left Jacob in the corridor and went up the stairs.

---

The neurology department occupied the third floor's eastern wing. At 2:20 AM it held two nurses at the central station, one resident in the on-call room at the corridor's far end, and the institutional silence of a ward where the patients were sedated and the staff had reached the point in the night shift where conversation required more energy than it returned.

Hayden moved through it the way he moved through every occupied space. Not hurrying. Not hesitating. The white coat he'd taken from the locker room on the ground floor was two sizes too large across the shoulders, but nobody examined the fit of a coat at 2:20 AM.

Dr. Sarah Winters occupied office 3-14. Her name was on the door in the standard hospital font, below her title: *Consultant Neurologist, Quantum Adjacency Disorders Unit.*

The lock was a standard hospital keycard system — not QSA-adjacent, not biometric. He had the master override on a card he'd cloned from the facilities management system three weeks ago, during a reconnaissance transition he'd made specifically for this purpose.

The door opened. He went in. He closed it behind him.

---

The office held the accumulated disorder of someone who worked in it rather than performed in it. Files on the desk, physical and digital both. A whiteboard along the left wall covered in notation that was half medical shorthand and half something that looked, to Hayden's eye, like the early stages of a theoretical framework — the kind of notation that appeared when a researcher was working at the edge of what the existing vocabulary could describe and had begun inventing new symbols for the concepts that didn't have names yet.

He photographed the whiteboard first. Then the desk files.

Then he opened her terminal.

The QSA's civilian medical data-sharing protocols gave Winters's unit access to the agency's anonymized PQ genotype database — the research cohort the QSA maintained for studying quantum adjacency disorders in the general population. Winters had been publishing from this database for six years. Her most recent paper, eighteen months ago, had described a treatment protocol for early-stage Bleed that achieved a 34 percent reduction in fragmentation frequency in a cohort of twelve patients.

Hayden had read the paper four times.

He had read it because the treatment protocol described in section four — the coherence stabilization approach, the combination of targeted quantum field exposure and cellular-level calcium chelation — was the closest thing to a functional ICS recovery method that existed in the published literature. Not a cure. The paper was careful about that. Winters was careful about everything. But a stabilization. A slowing of the decline. A method that might, if applied to a subject whose ICS had not yet crossed the threshold below which the protocols changed their name, extend the operational window by a factor the paper estimated, conservatively, at between eight and fourteen months.

Eight to fourteen months.

His ICS was thirty-four. The threshold was forty. He had crossed it six days ago, when the reading dropped from forty-one to thirty-seven, and he had not told anyone because there was no one to tell and because telling someone would have required explaining what he was and what he had done and what the four hundred and twelve transitions had cost, and that was a conversation that ended in one of two ways and he had no interest in either.

The paper described twelve patients. The database held the anonymized records of all twelve. What the paper did not contain — what no published paper could contain, because the ethics protocols governing the QSA's civilian research database prohibited the inclusion of identifying information in any published work — was the treatment methodology in sufficient detail to replicate it without Winters's direct involvement.

That methodology was in her unpublished notes.

He found them in the third subdirectory of her research partition, behind a password that took him four minutes to break — not because it was sophisticated, but because his hands had developed, over the past three months, a tendency to miskey under pressure. The coherence calcification in the arterial walls had begun to affect the fine motor control in his fingers. He had adapted. He typed more slowly now, checked each entry before confirming it, and had learned to treat the extra time as a cost of operation rather than a failure of execution.

He copied the directory to the encrypted drive. All of it — the notes, the raw data, the correspondence with the QSA's research liaison, the draft of the follow-up paper Winters had not yet submitted and that contained, in its methodology section, the treatment parameters the published version had omitted.

The copy took six minutes. He stood at the terminal and watched the progress bar and listened to the ward's silence and counted his pulse at the carotid.

Seventy-nine. Down from ninety-one. On schedule.

The ICS monitor pulsed once behind his left eye. He did not look at the reading.

---

He was back in the service corridor at 2:41 AM. Jacob was where he'd left him — breathing steady, position unchanged, the shift log tablet on the floor beside his right hand. Hayden set the radio down next to it. Jacob would wake in approximately twenty minutes with a headache concentrated in the left shoulder and neck and no clear memory of the preceding two minutes. He would file an incident report. The incident report would describe an unscheduled individual in the service corridor. The description would be accurate and useless.

Hayden went out through the service entrance and into the car park.

---

The transition point was the maintenance corridor behind the eastern substation. He had forty minutes before the B-734 insertion window closed — the coherence alignment between baseline and B-734 held for ninety minutes at this geographic location, and he had used fifty of them.

He stood in the corridor and prepared.

Preparation was not a ritual. It was a medical procedure he performed on himself, without equipment, in the dark. He checked his pulse. Seventy-eight — baseline for post-transition recovery, which meant his body had stabilized from the inbound crossing and was operating at its current normal, which was not the same as healthy but was the best available approximation. He flexed his fingers — all ten, in sequence, checking the fine motor response. The ring and little finger of his right hand lagged by approximately forty milliseconds. Consistent with the last six transitions. Not worsening.

He checked the ICS monitor.

*32.*

Two points in forty minutes. The transition stress was accelerating the decline. He had known this was possible — Winters's notes described it, in the section on advanced-stage coherence degradation, as *transition-induced cascade*: the phenomenon by which the quantum state disruption of a crossing event destabilized the body's remaining coherence faster than the recovery period could compensate. Below a certain ICS threshold, each transition cost more than the interval between transitions could restore.

He was below that threshold. He had been below it for six days.

He ran the arithmetic one more time because running it was the habit and habits were what remained when the reasoning had worn smooth. Thirty-two. The threshold below which the clinical protocols changed their language. In those six days he had continued the operation because the operation required continuation and because the alternative — reporting the ICS reading to the QSA medical division and entering the stand-down process — carried a known outcome. Stand-down at ICS 32 meant assessment. Assessment meant documentation. Documentation meant the operational window closed and the Winters methodology stayed in Winters's partition where it was inaccessible to the one person who had concrete reason to read it.

Thirty-two was not a number he had arrived at by accident. It was the number the calculation produced when you ran it honestly against the data. He had run it honestly. The calculation was sound. He was below the threshold and the mission was not complete and both of those facts were equally true and he had decided, six days ago and confirmed each day since, that they were not equally weighted.

The ozone came first.

He had not registered the ozone during previous transitions. He registered it now — a sharper note under the standard corridor smell, chemical and close, the smell of a process running at a frequency his body had no prior category for.

He filed it. Initiated the transition.

The sound arrived below hearing — a frequency the body registered as pressure rather than noise, a compression that began at the sternum and moved outward through the ribcage and into the arms and legs and finally into the skull, where it became, for approximately three seconds, the agony of every sinus cavity attempting to equalize against a pressure differential that had no physical source. He had learned, across four hundred and twelve crossings, not to fight it. Fighting it extended the three seconds. Accepting it did not shorten them, but it preserved the muscle control he needed for what came after.

The pressure peaked. His vision went — not dark, not white, but absent, the visual cortex temporarily decoupled from its input as the quantum state transfer moved through the neurological architecture. He counted. One. Two. Three.

The weight of the air changed.

B-734's 0.3 percent gravitational excess hit his legs first — the familiar, unwelcome heaviness, the floor suddenly more present than it had been, the body's recalibration beginning its ninety-second process. His vision returned in sections: peripheral field first, then centre, the maintenance corridor assembling itself around him in the copper-and-standing-water air of a branch reality that had been running parallel to baseline for longer than anyone currently alive had been alive.

He was through.

His legs reported. Not pain — the proprioceptive system's clinical notification that the floor's position had been confirmed and the weight distribution was wrong. The 0.3 percent gravitational excess arriving in the musculature's load calculation as a deficit in the body's margin, the legs finding themselves carrying more than the motor cortex had prepared for. The recalibration began its ninety-second sequence and the legs, mid-sequence, stopped receiving the instructions that sequence required.

He went down.

Not dramatically. The way an unsupported object goes down when the supporting structure is removed: without agency, without resistance, the concrete arriving at a predetermined rate and meeting the body's mass with the indifference of physics. His left knee absorbed the first impact. His right palm took the secondary. He stayed there.

The ICS monitor pulsed behind his left eye.

*32.*

He counted his pulse at the carotid. Ninety-one. His breathing was intact. His vision was intact — the maintenance corridor present, the conduit on the upper wall, the copper smell, the standing water sound from somewhere below the substation housing. B-734's maintenance corridor, assembled correctly around him, all of it in its documented position.

His right hand was not there.

He looked at the concrete where his right hand should have been — where his right palm had taken the secondary impact, where the topography of that hand's contact with the floor should have registered as sensation. The floor was there. The hand was not. No pain. No transition residue. The absence was absolute and without explanation, the way an object is absent when it has simply ceased to occupy the space it had been occupying.

He looked at the space for four seconds.

Then he saw it.

Not in B-734. In baseline — the maintenance corridor behind the Alfred's eastern substation, the corridor he had left forty-three minutes ago when he initiated the transit. The hand was there: pressed flat against the corridor's concrete wall, the right palm against the painted surface, the same pressure distribution as an anchor grip, the hand doing what his hands did under neurological stress without being told to. The fingers were his fingers. The pressure was the response of his nervous system to a collapse it was managing alone, split across two realities simultaneously, the body's distress signal arriving in the only location that portion of his architecture currently occupied.

He watched it for three seconds.

Then it was back. His right hand on the concrete floor of B-734's maintenance corridor, present and weight-bearing and registering the cold and the aggregate in the surface with the full fidelity of functional sensation.

The ICS monitor did not pulse again.

He stayed on the floor and counted his pulse at the carotid until the ninety-second recalibration completed and his legs were his legs again and the proprioceptive map had updated to B-734's gravitational constant and the ozone note — the additional note under the standard corridor smell — had faded to a trace that could have been anything.

He pushed off the floor with both hands.

Both hands present. Both hands functional. The fine motor response in the ring and little finger of the right hand lagged by forty milliseconds, consistent with the last six transitions. Not worsening.

He noted this. He noted the absence. He noted the duration — three seconds — and the location the hand had appeared and what it had been doing there.

He did not note these things in the jump log. He noted them in the partition he had been building since jump 289, the private record that ran parallel to the clinical documentation, the record of the things that did not have a clinical category yet.

He had now seen where a piece of him went.

The encrypted drive was in his jacket pocket. Winters's notes. He had forty minutes before the B-734 monitoring cycle ran its passive scan. He needed to be out of the corridor and into the safe house before then.

His pulse: seventy-nine. Coming down.

He pushed off the wall and walked.

<a name="chapter-7"></a> [home](#home)

# CHAPTER 7 — THE APARTMENT

The intake station occupied the last twenty metres of the Threshold facility's exit corridor — a narrowing that was architectural rather than accidental, designed so nothing left the building without passing through the sensor array embedded in both walls. The array read. It did not ask.

The technician behind the desk held out her right hand, palm up, without breaking attention from her terminal. A woman in her thirties with the economy of movement that belonged to people who performed the same precise actions many times a day and had eliminated everything unnecessary.

Kain placed his wrist against the scanner. Warm from continuous use. The read ran four seconds — standard biometric sweep, pulse and temperature and the subcutaneous markers the QSA used to track personnel movement through secured facilities.

Then the PQ genotype flag.

The technician's eyes moved — not to him, not to the terminal, but to a point approximately thirty centimetres to the left of both. The specific unfocused gaze of someone processing data they had not expected and deciding what to do with it.

One second. Two.

She withdrew the scanner. Her eyes returned to the terminal. Her fingers moved across the input surface with the same precise economy as before, logging the exit, completing the record.

"Clear," she said.

He walked through.

The corridor beyond the intake station ran twelve metres to the facility's outer door. He walked it at the same pace he had walked every other corridor in the building — the pace of someone with somewhere to be and no reason to hurry. His right hand rode in his jacket pocket, thumb against the seam. The tremor ran its low, reliable frequency.

Behind him, the technician's terminal now carried a flag in his biometric record. And whoever accessed the intake logs would see it.

He filed this and kept walking.

***

The QSA's surveillance team had preserved Chen's apartment with the thoroughness of people who understood that a space's information held as much value as its objects. Nothing cleaned. Nothing rearranged since the team's last documented sweep. The kitchen counter held the ring from a mug set down and not retrieved. The living room carpet showed the compression patterns of furniture that had not shifted in the weeks since the replacement took occupancy — the same positions as the original layout, maintained with the precise institutional caution of something operating from a documented record rather than from habit.

Kain walked through without touching anything.

The reading pass first. The pass where you let the space speak before you imposed questions on it. He had developed it on the Varn case and refined it across the subsequent years in the smaller, less sanctioned work — the missing persons, the insurance fraud, the cases where the budget held a fixed number and intuition served as the only renewable resource. Stripped of everything institutional, the pass had become faster and more accurate. Necessity, clarifying.

The apartment's geometry ran open-plan: kitchen and living area sharing the southern portion, a partial wall separating the bedroom, the bathroom beyond it. North-facing windows. The Melbourne skyline in the flat register of a winter morning, mid-rise buildings and the passive architecture of the city's interior grid. The replacement had occupied the space systematically. Furniture at the documented positions. Personal effects in their recorded places. The passive surveillance baseline — shower times, movement patterns, the seventeen behaviours the QSA monitored as standard — preserved and continued.

Almost.

He stopped at the kitchen table.

The chair sat pushed back at approximately forty degrees from the table's edge, in the position of a chair vacated and not returned to — unremarkable. The direction it faced carried the weight. The chair faced the apartment's entry door, the occupant's back to the kitchen wall.

Chen had worked in surveillance-adjacent environments for eleven years. Civilians who worked in those environments long enough developed certain spatial habits, not through training but through exposure: the preference for seating that placed the door in the forward visual field, the automatic positioning of the body to observe entry points. Not protocol. Sediment. The way a decade of paying attention to who entered rooms accumulated into a disposition about where you sat in them.

Kain's reading: the replacement had positioned the chair facing the door because the documentation of Chen's office seating showed a man who faced the door. What the documentation could not convey — what no document could convey — was that the man who faced the door at work had spent eleven years needing to stop working when he came home. To sit with his back to the door and let the room's entrance fall out of his immediate sensory field. A civilian space with your back to the wall marked where you stopped being operational. Chen had needed that. The replacement had not known to need it, and so the chair faced the door.

He photographed it. Moved on.

The kitchen bench held the kettle on the right side of the burner unit. The QSA's layout documentation — compiled from the passive surveillance array, which tracked movement and biometric deviation but not the fine domestic geography of a person's dominant hand — showed the kettle on the right. That documentation captured the replacement's arrangement, not Chen's. The array had no positional baseline from before the substitution. It recorded what it found.

Chen's QSA personnel file carried a medical notation: left-hand dominant, fine motor. A left-handed person who made tea or coffee multiple times a day placed the kettle on the left side of the bench, reaching for it without looking, the motion so practised that the reasoning had long since dissolved. The replacement had inherited the array's record. The array's record reflected the replacement's own placement. The kettle sat on the right.

He photographed it. Moved to the window.

The blind hung raised to the fifth slat from the bottom. The window sill carried three years of dust accumulation, and in the dust, the faint lateral marks of fingertip contact — a wear pattern at the height of the third slat, not the fifth, left by a finger that had touched the same point on the same slat enough times that the contact had become a groove. Chen raised the blind to the third slat every morning. The replacement had raised it to the fifth — more light, a more optimal position by any documented standard, and wrong in a way that no document could have told it.

He photographed the wear mark. He photographed the blind's current position.

Three residues. The QSA's instruments had measured shower durations and movement frequencies and the spatial geometry of the book on the shelf. They had produced three anomalies of their own. He had produced three more in eight minutes by standing in a room and looking at where things sat.

He moved to the bedroom.

The books on the nightstand matched the documentation: a novel, a volume of essays, a journal that the QSA had logged as a personal record and had not accessed on the grounds that a personal record remained a personal record. The novel's spine showed the stress fractures of a book read multiple times — the specific surface damage of repeated openings, the pages expanded by humidity and use. He picked it up. Set it back. The essay volume beside it carried comparable condition.

The third book differed.

A slim volume — not the journal, the journal ran standard ruled notebook, this held a published cover that had lost its original colour in the specific way that books lost colour when they were read outside, taken to parks and beaches and the kind of places where light fell on them at variable angles for years. The book had lived outdoors. Chen had owned it for a long time.

A single page, dog-eared.

The dog-ear ran recent. The crease held sharp, the fold not yet absorbed into the page's general wear — a corner turned within the last weeks, the paper still holding the exact angle of the fold rather than the softened suggestion of a fold made long ago. He opened to the page.

An underline on page 211. A single sentence, underlined in pencil, the pencil strokes faded to grey: the mark of a much earlier reading, returned to.

*The only proof of identity that cannot be forged is the proof that others cannot see.*

He read it twice. The sentence occupied the middle of a paragraph about bureaucratic records — the book a history of institutional documentation practices, the kind of text that accumulated underlining in graduate seminars and then travelled through a working life, consulted at intervals. The underline belonged to an earlier Chen, a version working through something academic who had recognised this sentence as important in a way that had not yet become personal.

The dog-ear belonged to the version of Chen who had needed to find it again quickly.

He stood with the book in both hands and ran the arithmetic. The dog-ear, fresh. The dog-ear, on this page. This page carrying this sentence about identity and proof. Chen had returned to this sentence in his last days, before the replacement — before he understood what approached, or in the first hours of understanding it. He had turned down the corner of this page and then he had done the other things: the 7:15 tram, the reversed spine on *The Maltese Falcon*, the schedule. He had left a cache of signals for anyone who could read the negative space, and buried inside that cache, in a book the QSA had logged and not opened, sat this.

*The only proof of identity that cannot be forged is the proof that others cannot see.*

Chen had understood the residue theory before Solis named it. Or he had understood it the same way Solis had — not as a theory but as a lived fact, arrived at from the inside.

Kain photographed the page.

The Bleed arrived at the bedroom doorway.

Not the full event — the precursor. The specific neurological pressure that preceded a fragmentation episode: the TUBA1A-PQ binding sites registering the apartment's accumulated sensory residue, the biophotonic trace of three years of a specific person's daily occupation pressing against the dormant architecture. A space this thoroughly inhabited by someone now gone carried a coherence weight that standard environments did not. He stood at the doorway and held the book and pressed two fingers to the inside of his left wrist.

Sixty-eight. Elevated.

The peripheral field held. No stutter. He counted twelve seconds in the doorway and the precursor did not escalate and he moved back into the living room.

He replaced the book on the nightstand. Not in the dog-eared position — he smoothed the corner carefully back to a close approximation of unread and returned the book to its documented position. The photograph lived on his device. The book stayed.

He went through the living room once more, slowly, from the door to the window. Looking for the thing that did not sit right, the residue of the residue — the sense that the space had delivered most of what it carried but held one more thing at an angle he had not yet found.

He did not find it. Some rooms kept one thing. He filed the absence and moved to the door.

---

***

He sat in Betty for eleven minutes before contacting Emily.

Not because the eleven minutes served a necessary function. Because the eleven minutes formed the interval between what he had found and what he intended to give her — the gap in which the two things separated into their final categories. The three chair-and-kettle-and-blind observations: hers. The photograph of the wear mark: hers. The dog-eared page: his. For now. Not permanently — the dog-ear would enter the report eventually, when he understood what Chen had been telling whoever found it and whether that understanding belonged to the investigation or to something the investigation occupied without knowing it yet.

He called Emily.

She picked up on the first ring.

"Three additional residues," he said. "Chair orientation, kettle placement, window blind wear. I'll send the photographs."

A pause. "That's good work."

"Chen returned to a book in his last days. A history of document practices, page 211. Pencil underline, old. Dog-ear, recent." He did not send the photograph. "I need access to his reading history for the last six months. Any QSA library borrowing, any civilian purchases logged to his registered accounts."

"I can get that by morning."

"Thank you."

He ended the call and sat in the car and looked at the building's entry and thought about a man who had turned down the corner of a page about identity and proof and had then gone to his 7:15 tram and reversed his book spine and drunk his black coffee and left every small signal he could in the hours before the replacement took occupancy and his own signals became someone else's.

He started Betty's engine.

The city received him back without comment, the way cities received people at mid-morning on weekdays: as one more vehicle among the thousands operating within the normal parameters of a day that was not, for most of those thousands, the kind of day this was.  

He drove.

<a name="chapter-8"></a> [home](#home)

# CHAPTER 8 — THE VECTOR

The QSA field office occupied the third floor of a building designed to look like it served no particular purpose. Glass and brushed aluminium, indistinguishable from the financial services firms on either side. The lobby receptionist directed visitors to floors one and two with the practiced efficiency of someone who had never been asked about floor three. The elevator required a keycard not issued to visitors. The stairwell door on the third floor required a second—Level 4 clearance minimum.

Kain had Level 4 clearance. He had not slept in thirty-one hours.

The ninety minutes in the car outside the Solis building did not constitute sleep in any clinical sense. His nervous system had entered a shallow holding pattern—a temporary reduction in the rate at which it was consuming itself. The Bleed's 1.4x multiplier existed as a physical reality: the fluorescent lighting in the stairwell arrived at his eyes a fraction of a second after it should have, as though the signal travelled through a medium slightly denser than air. His depth perception was off by a margin too small to measure and too large to ignore. The third step from the top of the stairwell landing looked closer than it was. He adjusted his stride without breaking it.

He pushed through the stairwell door and walked toward the briefing room at the end of the corridor.

Emily was already there.

She sat at the table with a coffee she had not touched, her fingers wrapped around the cup the way people hold things when they need something to do with their hands. 

He clocked the cup before her face.

The briefing room ran its air conditioning for the server rack in the adjacent equipment bay—calibrated for hardware, not people. He had been in this room enough times to know the rate at which a hot liquid cooled under those conditions: from coffee temperature to drinkable in under four minutes, from drinkable to room temperature in twelve. The surface of the liquid in Emily's cup was not steaming. It was not moving. It had the specific stillness of a liquid that had achieved thermal equilibrium with a room kept at equipment temperature.

She had been here for at least twelve minutes before he arrived.

The expression that crossed her face when he entered—relief, followed immediately by the suppression of relief—lasted approximately half a second before she replaced it with something more neutral. The suppression was its own kind of information.

The server rack pushed a low-frequency vibration through the shared wall — below the threshold of conscious hearing, but present in the sternum as a faint pressure

"You look terrible," she said.

"I found something in B-734." He sat down across from her and placed the data chip on the table between them. "Chen's terminal had a secondary access log. Someone ran a query through the hospital's civilian research database three days before Chen was replaced."

Emily's eyes moved to the chip, then back to him. "What kind of query?"

"PQ Genotype research cohort. Specifically, the anonymised patient files connected to a neurologist named Sarah Winters. The query pulled her entire research dataset—methodology, patient outcomes, unpublished findings." He paused. "The search parameters were precise. Not exploratory."

"That's not a casual breach," she said.

"No."

She picked up her coffee, held it, set it back down without drinking. The cup left a ring on the brushed steel table. She did not wipe it. "Winters is QSA-adjacent. Her research is civilian, but the cohort data is flagged under the medical data-sharing protocol. Accessing it requires a Level 3 clearance minimum."

"Or a cloned keycard and six minutes with the central server."

The silence that followed contained more than one conversation. Emily's eyes moved to the chip, then to a point slightly to his left—not the wall, not him. The server rack hummed through the wall. The fluorescent tube above the table had a barely perceptible flicker, a cycle of approximately four seconds, that his sleep-deprived visual cortex had locked onto and could not release.

"The network is interested in Winters's research," she said.

"The network is interested in what Winters's research proves." He kept his voice level. "There's a difference."

"Which is?"

"Winters has been documenting PQ Genotype expression in civilian carriers for eleven years. Unpublished. The QSA knows the research exists but hasn't moved to classify it because classifying it would require acknowledging that the civilian carrier population is larger than the official figures suggest." He let that land. "Someone outside the QSA just pulled that data. Someone who already knew it existed and knew where to find it."

Emily's fingers spread on the table, then returned. "That's a significant operational capability."

"Yes."

"It suggests resources. Infrastructure. A network that's been building for longer than the current anomaly sequence."

"Yes."

She looked at him directly. "What else did you find?"

The question was precise. Not *did you find anything else*—which would have been casual, exploratory—but *what else*, which assumed the existence of additional information and asked him to produce it. He had been waiting for that question since he walked through the door. He had been deciding, since before he walked through the door, exactly how to answer it.

"The replacement's behavioural pattern in B-734 is consistent with the other three anomalies," he said. "Same methodology. Same error signature. Same window of operational deployment before the errors compound." He paused. "Four anomalies with identical methodology suggests a single architect. Someone who built the replacement protocol and is running it across multiple realities simultaneously."

"That's what the evidence supports," Emily said.

"That's what the evidence supports," he agreed.

He did not tell her about the journals. He did not tell her that he knew she had taken them from Solis's apartment before the sequestration, that he had found the gap in the evidence chain where the journals should have been and traced the gap back to her. He did not tell her that the journals were the piece that connected the replacement methodology to its origin point, and that whoever held the journals held the thread that unravelled the entire network.

He did not tell her because she already knew all of it, and telling her would tell her that he knew, and knowing that he knew would change the geometry of everything that followed.

Instead, he said, "I need to get to Winters before the network does."

"She'll be at Alfred on Tuesday morning." Emily said it without the micro-pause that preceded genuine recall—the cadence of something already decided. "Research clinic."

"Today is Tuesday."

"Yes."

He stood up from the table. The movement cost him more than it should have—a half-second lag between the decision to stand and the execution of it, his body running behind his intentions in the way that happened when the Bleed was active and the neural pathways operated at reduced efficiency. He covered it by reaching for the data chip, a purposeful movement that explained the pause.

Emily's attention did not move from his face. She had the particular quality of focus that came from knowing someone well enough to read the gaps in their performance—the places where the presentation and the reality diverged. He had seen her apply that attention to other people. He had not, until recently, understood that she had been applying it to him.

"When did you last sleep?" she asked.

"I'm fine."

"That's not what I asked."

"It's the answer I have." He pocketed the chip. "I'll contact you from Alfred."

"Kain."

He stopped at the door.

"The network moved on Winters's data three days ago," she said. "If they're following the same pattern as the other anomalies, they've already made their next move. You won't be tracking them. You'll be following them."

"I know."

"Then you know that going to Alfred without knowing what you're walking into is—"

"Necessary," he said. "It's necessary."

He left before she could find a word for what it actually was.

---

The Bleed hit him in the stairwell.

He was on the landing between the second and third floors when it arrived—a hard interruption, a cut in the continuity of sensory experience so complete that the four steps preceding it ceased to exist in his memory. One moment he was pushing through the stairwell door at the top. The next he was standing on the landing with his right hand on the railing and no account of how it had gotten there.

The sound came before anything else.

Not from behind his eyes. From ahead of him—two metres forward on the landing, at the precise position between the stairwell door and the handrail where a person would stand if they were waiting for someone ascending. Directional. Located. The specificity was the wrong part, the part that sent the body's error-correction mechanism into its failure loop: sounds originating inside the skull were categorised as neurological events and filed accordingly. A sound with a position in space—a sound that arrived from a point two metres ahead, from a height consistent with a person standing at the landing—was not a neurological event. It was an acoustic signal from a source that the visual cortex, scanning the landing, confirmed was not there.

A woman laughing. High, uncontrolled. The laugh of someone who had found something genuinely funny in a situation that contained nothing funny. It lasted three seconds—he counted them—and it did not stop so much as withdraw, pulling back through the architecture of the stairwell the way sound pulled back when its source moved away, leaving the texture of itself behind at the two-metre mark.

He pressed his right hand flat against the railing.

The metal was cold and solid and vibrating faintly with the building's ventilation system below. He held the vibration and counted it separate from his pulse and let both of them run until the laughing woman was not present at the two-metre mark and had not been present for nine consecutive seconds—the interval he had established, across four years of managing this, as the minimum duration of absence before an episode was over rather than paused.

Nine seconds. Then three more for margin.

He stood on the landing and did not move.

The stairwell existed around him in two registers simultaneously. The first was the physical reality of the building: the hum of the ventilation system, the distant sound of Commercial Road traffic filtering through the concrete, the fluorescent tube above the landing buzzing at a frequency slightly higher than the one on the third floor. The second was a pressure at the periphery of his visual field, a sense of additional space that the stairwell did not contain—the ghost of a room that was not this room pressing itself against the edges of his perception.

B-734.

The dormant PQ architecture in his neural tissue had no mechanism for sorting the sensory data it absorbed during a transition. An active carrier—someone whose architecture had been conditioned through the QSA's protocols—maintained a functional boundary between baseline reality and the residual imprints of the realities they passed through. His architecture was dormant. The boundary it maintained was the boundary of a structure that had never been designed for load-bearing. The Sphere's entanglement field had forced it into service anyway, and the microscopic scarring in the microtubule structures that resulted from that forced activation did not heal between exposures. It accumulated. Each episode of scarring widened the gaps in the boundary. Each gap allowed more residual data through.

The laughing woman was not a hallucination. She was a memory that did not belong to him—a fragment of B-734's sensory record that had adhered to his neural tissue during the transition and was now surfacing through the damaged pathways at intervals he could not predict and could not control. The 1.4x multiplier from sleep deprivation did not create the damage. It accelerated the rate at which the existing damage expressed itself. It thinned the boundary further, made the gaps wider, reduced the interval between episodes.

He counted his pulse against the railing. Eighty-nine beats per minute. Elevated, but not critically. His vision had stabilised—the ghost of the additional room had receded to a faint pressure at the far edge of his peripheral field, present but not dominant. The laughing woman had not returned.

He descended the remaining stairs and pushed through the lobby door into the morning light.

---

Alfred Hospital was twenty minutes south in ordinary traffic. The traffic on St Kilda Road at eight-fifteen on a Tuesday morning was the specific density of a city that had decided, collectively, to be somewhere else at the same time—a compression of vehicles that moved in increments of three to five metres before stopping again, each stop accompanied by engines idling at the edge of their patience.

The rain had come in off the bay sometime in the last hour. A fine, persistent mist that the windscreen wipers handled at their lowest setting—a slow sweep every four seconds that cleared the glass and watched it fog again. The city outside the windows was the grey of wet concrete and brake lights, the kind of grey that flattened depth and made distances unreliable.

Kain drove with both hands on the wheel and his attention divided in a way that was not safe and could not be corrected. The primary division was between the road and the data chip in his jacket pocket—not the chip itself, but what it represented, the thread it pulled on, the specific shape of the network's interest in Winters's research. The secondary division was between the road and the stairwell landing, the laughing woman, the ghost of the additional room pressing against the edges of his visual field.

The traffic light at the intersection of St Kilda Road and Domain Road cycled through green while he was still four cars back. He registered the green. The four seconds between the green and his foot moving from the brake to the accelerator were four seconds he could not fully account for—not a gap, not a blackout, but a thinning of attention so complete that the interval had passed without leaving a clear record.

The car behind him sounded its horn.

He moved through the intersection.

The 1.4x multiplier was not a number. It was the specific experience of operating a vehicle through Melbourne rain while a portion of his cognitive architecture was occupied with sensory data from a reality he had left eight hours ago. It was his depth perception arriving at conclusions slightly after the situations that had prompted them. It was the experience of knowing, with clinical precision, exactly what was happening to him and being unable to stop it, because stopping it required sleep and sleep required time and time was the one resource the network was not going to give him.

He parked on a side street two blocks from the hospital and sat in the car for ninety seconds with the engine off, running a manual inventory of his current functional state. Vision: stable, depth perception degraded by an estimated eight percent. Auditory: clear, no residual intrusions in the last eleven minutes. Cognition: sequential reasoning intact, working memory showing gaps at the margins. Motor: fine motor control within acceptable parameters, gross motor slightly delayed.

Acceptable, if not good.

He got out of the car and walked toward the hospital in the rain.

The street folded. The wet bitumen of the side street ceased to be the surface under his feet and became the linoleum of the Sydney corridor, the overhead fluorescents replacing the sodium streetlights in a single frame-cut that his visual cortex registered as a physical blow behind the left eye.

He stopped walking.

His right hand went to his jacket pocket. Not a decision—a reflex, the same reflex that had sent his hand to the same pocket every day for nine years without producing anything useful. The lighter was there. It was always there. His fingers closed around it: the rectangular weight, the worn brass casing, the engraving on the face worn smooth enough that he had to press his thumbnail into the letters to read them by touch. *N.K. — Solis — Case 7.* The flint wheel sat under his thumb, ridged and familiar.

He rolled it.

The dry rasp of metal on spent flint. The faint chemical ghost of fuel that had evaporated sometime in the previous decade. No spark. No catch. The same result it had produced every time he'd tested it in nine years, which was not often, because he did not smoke, had not smoked since the week after Solis handed it to him across the table at the Punt Road debrief and said *keep it, you earned it, and you should quit anyway.*

He had quit. He had kept the lighter. He had never replaced the flint.

The Melbourne street reasserted itself. The fluorescents dissolved back into sodium. The linoleum became bitumen.

He stood in the rain with the dead lighter in his fist and waited for his visual field to stabilise.

Forty-three seconds. Then the overlay was gone.

He put the lighter back in his pocket and kept walking.

---

The main atrium of Alfred Hospital operated at a frequency that had nothing to do with sound. It was a frequency of human density—the compression of bodies moving through a space designed for throughput rather than comfort, each body carrying its own particular weight of urgency or dread or the exhaustion of people who had been here before and knew what waiting in this building meant.

The smell arrived before the visual data resolved. Antiseptic over something organic that the antiseptic had not fully covered, and beneath both, the staleness of air that had been breathed by too many people in too short a time. His olfactory system processed it and cross-referenced it against the residual sensory data from B-734, where the hospital had smelled almost identical—the same antiseptic, the same organic undertone, the same staleness—but with a fractional difference in the chemical composition of the cleaning products that his nervous system had catalogued during the transition and had not yet released.

The atrium ceiling rose four stories, glass and steel, the morning's grey light pressing through in a diffuse wash that eliminated shadows and made everyone in the space look slightly unwell. A coffee kiosk near the main entrance generated a queue of eight. A family of four occupied a row of chairs near the information desk, the youngest child asleep across two seats, the adults arranged in the specific geometry of people who had been sitting in the same configuration for long enough that the configuration had become load-bearing.

Kain moved through the atrium toward the elevator bank and did not stop moving.

The sensory density of the space pressed against the thinned boundary in his neural architecture with a persistence that required active management. Every sound—the PA system announcing a name, the wheels of a trolley on polished linoleum, the acoustic signature of a space where sound bounced off hard surfaces and arrived from multiple directions simultaneously—carried the potential to trigger a cross-reference with B-734's residual data. He managed it the way he managed everything: by keeping his attention narrow, his focus specific, his cognitive resources allocated to the immediate task rather than the peripheral noise.

The elevator arrived. He stepped in. The doors closed.

In the silence of the elevator, the laughing woman returned for exactly two seconds.

He pressed the button for the second floor and did not move until the doors opened.

---

The research clinic occupied a wing on the hospital's second floor, accessible through a corridor that branched off the main thoroughfare and was marked with signage deliberately understated—a small placard, white text on grey, that read NEUROLOGICAL RESEARCH — LEVEL 2 in a font size suggesting the clinic had no interest in visitors who had not already been told where to find it.

The corridor was empty at eight forty-seven in the morning. Three doors on the left side, each closed. A nurses' station at the far end, unmanned. A window at the corridor's midpoint looked out over the hospital's internal courtyard—a square of grey concrete with a single tree that had been planted with optimism and had responded with the kind of survival indistinguishable from resignation.

He stopped at the nurses' station and looked at the appointment board.

Winters's name appeared twice. The first entry was at nine o'clock—a patient appointment, the name redacted under the privacy protocol. The second was at ten-thirty, marked not as a patient appointment but as a data review session. The notation beside it read: *W. methodology cross-ref — see attached protocol.*

He looked at the notation for a long time.

The replacement's behavioural pattern across all four anomalies included a specific error: the tendency to maintain the replaced individual's external commitments while subtly altering the internal methodology. The meetings stayed on the calendar. The signatures stayed consistent. But the underlying logic of the decisions shifted—small deviations that accumulated over days until the pattern became visible to someone who knew what to look for.

*W. methodology cross-ref* was not language Winters would use to describe her own work. It was the language of someone describing Winters's work from the outside—someone who had read the research dataset pulled from Chen's terminal and was now attempting to replicate the methodology without fully understanding it.

The replacement had already been here.

He turned from the appointment board and pulled out his phone. He needed to know when the data breach at Chen's terminal had occurred relative to Winters's last confirmed appearance at the clinic. He needed to know whether the person who had attended the nine o'clock appointment this morning was Sarah Winters or someone who had spent three days learning to be her.

He dialled Emily.

She picked up on the second ring.

"The replacement is already operational," he said. "I need Winters's biometric entry log for the last four days. Every time she badged into this building."

A pause. Not the pause of someone who needed time to process the request—the pause of someone deciding how much of their own information to reveal in response to his. "I can get that."

"How long?"

"Twenty minutes."

"I'll be in the atrium."

He ended the call and stood in the corridor for a moment, looking at the appointment board, at the notation that was not Winters's language, at the evidence of a replacement that had been operational for long enough to schedule its own data review sessions.

The laughing woman did not return.

He descended to the atrium and found a chair with a clear sightline to the elevator bank and the main entrance and sat down to wait. The rain continued outside the glass ceiling. The family near the information desk maintained their load-bearing geometry. His nervous system continued its slow, measurable work of consuming itself.

Twenty minutes. He had twenty minutes.

He used twelve of them before the next gap opened.

<a name="chapter-10"></a> [home](#home)

# **CHAPTER 10 — THE MIRROR HUNT**

Emily's call came at nine-fourteen.

Kain sat in the atrium, in the chair with the sightline to the elevator bank. A porter wheeled an empty gurney toward the service corridor with the particular unhurried efficiency of someone whose route had become muscular rather than cognitive. The atrium had filled in the twenty minutes since he had sat down — the morning's patient load arriving in the specific rhythm of a hospital that ran on appointment schedules, people moving through the space with the focused purposefulness of those who knew where they were going and the slower, more uncertain movement of those who did not.

He answered before the second ring.

"Winters badged in at seven fifty-two this morning," Emily said. No preamble. "That's forty-three minutes earlier than her average arrival time across the last eleven years of entry logs. The standard deviation on her arrival time is six minutes. Forty-three minutes is seven standard deviations outside her mean."

"What's her earliest recorded arrival?"

"Eight twenty-one. Three years ago. The day her research grant was approved."

Seven standard deviations. The number was not a statistical anomaly. It was a signature — the specific kind of error a replacement made when it had absorbed the subject's external schedule but not the internal logic that governed it. Winters arrived when her work required her to arrive. The replacement had arrived when its operational timeline required it to arrive, and had not understood that those two things were different.

"The nine o'clock appointment," Kain said.

"Booked four days ago through the hospital's internal scheduling system using Winters's credentials. The patient name is redacted, but the appointment type is listed as a follow-up consultation." Emily paused. "Winters doesn't do follow-up consultations. Her research clinic runs initial assessments and data collection. Follow-ups go to the clinical team."

"The replacement booked an appointment with its own network contact. Using Winters's system access to create a legitimate-looking meeting."

"That's the most probable interpretation."

"I need the floor plan for the second-floor research wing. Every exit, every camera position, every room that connects to the corridor outside the clinic."

"Sending now."

The floor plan arrived on his phone thirty seconds later — a clean architectural drawing annotated in Emily's hand with camera positions in red and exit routes in blue. He studied it for forty seconds, building the geometry of the space in his mind, mapping the replacement's most probable movement patterns against the available exits.

The replacement had arrived forty-three minutes early. The nine o'clock appointment had ended fourteen minutes ago. The replacement remained in the clinic, moved through the building, or had already vanished.

He stood and moved toward the elevator bank.

\*\*\*

Kain walked the corridor at the tempo of someone who belonged there — not hurried, not casual,the tempo of someone with a destination and a reason for it. He passed the nurses' station without stopping, his attention on the appointment board. The nine o'clock slot showed a check mark beside the redacted patient name. The ten-thirty data review session was unchanged.

Kain walked the corridor at the pace of someone who belonged there — not hurried, not casual,the tempo of someone with a destination and a reason for it. He passed the nurses' station without stopping, his attention on the appointment board. The nine o'clock slot showed a check mark beside the redacted patient name. The ten-thirty data review session was unchanged.

The nine o'clock appointment was complete. The replacement had been in this corridor within the last fourteen minutes.

He stopped at the window overlooking the internal courtyard and used his phone screen as a partial mirror to observe the corridor behind him without turning. The cleaner. The man with the visitor's lanyard. A nurse moving between the station and the far door. No one whose movement pattern carried the specific quality he was looking for.

The residue theory was not complicated in principle. A replacement absorbed the subject's external presentation — appearance, credentials, behavioral habits documented in observable records. What it could not absorb was the subject's relationship to physical space. The way a person moved through a familiar environment was the product of years of accumulated micro-decisions: which side of the corridor they favored, how they navigated around obstacles, the specific rhythm of their interaction with doors and equipment they had used hundreds of times. A replacement navigating Winters's environment for the first time moved through it with a fractional excess of attention — the attention of someone reading a space rather than inhabiting it.

He needed to find that excess.

He turned from the window and walked to the nurses' station.

The nurse behind the desk was in her mid-thirties, her attention split between a screen and a printed schedule she was annotating in pencil. She looked up when he reached the counter.

"I'm looking for Dr. Winters," he said. He produced the QSA field credential — not the full identification, which would trigger a reporting protocol, but the civilian liaison card that established him as a government health researcher. "I had a nine o'clock with her. I think I may have the wrong floor."

The nurse's expression moved through three registers in under two seconds: mild irritation at the interruption, the professional recalibration that followed, and then something more careful. "Dr. Winters's nine o'clock was in room two-fourteen. That's—" She stopped. "Can I ask what this is regarding?"

"Cohort data review. She's been consulting on a neurological study we're running through the health department."

The nurse looked at the schedule in front of her, then at the screen. "Dr. Winters checked out of the building at nine-twelve."

Nine-twelve. The appointment had ended at nine, or close to it. The replacement had been in the building for twelve minutes after the appointment concluded — long enough to remove the white coat, retrieve whatever it had come for, and execute the withdrawal.

"Did she say where she was going?"

"She doesn't usually—" The nurse stopped again. The careful expression returned, more pronounced this time. "Is there something wrong?"

"No," Kain said. "I must have the time wrong. I'll reschedule."

He moved away from the station before she could ask another question.

Room two-fourteen was the second door on the left — the one with the locked keycard reader he had noted on his first pass through the corridor. He stopped outside it and looked at the door without touching it. The keycard reader's indicator light showed green. Someone had unlocked it recently and not re-engaged the lock on exit.

He pushed the door open.

The room was small — a consultation space, not a research lab. Two chairs facing each other across a low table, a desk against the far wall with a monitor and keyboard, a single window overlooking the internal courtyard. The desk surface was clear except for a pen and a printed form that had been placed face-down. He turned the form over.

It was a standard patient intake document, blank except for a name in the top field: \*M. Reyes.\* No date of birth, no contact information, no medical history. The name had been written in a hand that was careful rather than natural — the deliberate letter formation of someone copying a signature they had practiced rather than producing their own.

He photographed the form and left the room.

\*\*\*

The staff corridor beyond the STAFF ONLY door was narrower, lower-ceilinged, the institutional green replaced with unpainted concrete. Service infrastructure ran along the upper walls — conduit, pipe, the bundled cabling of a building retrofitted with successive generations of technology without ever being fully rewired. The lighting was fluorescent and unshielded, tubes mounted directly to the ceiling without diffusers, producing a harsh, flat illumination that eliminated shadow and made the space feel simultaneously exposed and claustrophobic.

The elevator at the far end of the main corridor had opened while he was in room two-fourteen. He had not seen who stepped out. But the STAFF ONLY door showed the same tell as the keycard reader in two-fourteen — the mechanism not fully returned to its resting position, the door sitting a millimeter proud of the frame.

Someone had come through it in the last four minutes.

He pushed through into the staff corridor.

A woman stood twenty meters ahead of him.

She wore a white coat over dark clothing, a hospital ID badge on a lanyard, and carried a tablet in her left hand. Her hair was dark, pulled back, and her age was consistent with the photograph in Winters's personnel file. She moved down the corridor at a pace that was correct — not hurried, not uncertain — her attention on the tablet in her hand.

But her left hand was wrong.

He stopped walking.

The tell was in the grip orientation. He had learned to read grip orientation from the Varn case, where it was the security pass — right hand, badge facing out, six years of left-hand-facing-in dissolved into a replacement's approximation. The principle was the same: the body's relationship to a familiar object was not a decision. It was an accumulation. Four years of Winters holding a tablet meant four years of the same grip establishing itself in the musculature as a default, a pattern below the threshold of conscious selection, the kind of pattern that the replacement had been given as data but had not had time to absorb as habit.

You could tell a replacement the handedness. You could not give it the years.

The tablet was in her left hand, and she scrolled with her right thumb, which meant the tablet was oriented with the screen facing away from her body. Winters was left-handed. The personnel file noted it in the medical history section — a minor detail, the kind that appeared in records because it was clinically relevant to certain neurological assessments. A left-handed person holding a tablet in their left hand would orient it with the screen facing their body, scrolling with their left thumb or their right index finger. The orientation the woman used was the orientation of a right-handed person who had been told to carry the tablet in their left hand.

Kain moved after her.

She turned left at the end of the corridor, through a door marked STAFF ONLY that she opened with a keycard without breaking stride. He reached the door four seconds after it closed and used his Level 4 clearance to follow her through.

The corridor beyond was narrower still — a service passage, the walls bare concrete, the floor a rubberized surface that absorbed footsteps. The woman was fifteen meters ahead. He maintained the distance, keeping her in his peripheral vision rather than his direct sightline, moving with the same unhurried tempo she used.

She stopped.

Not the stop of someone who had reached a destination. The stop of someone who had registered a presence behind them and was deciding what to do about it. She stood with her back to him for three full seconds, the tablet still in her left hand, the screen still facing away from her body.

Then she turned around.

Her face was Winters's face — the same bone structure, the same coloring, the same arrangement of features that appeared in the personnel file photograph. But the expression was wrong. Winters's photograph showed a woman in her mid-forties with the particular quality of attention that came from years of research work — focused, slightly distracted, the expression of someone whose mind was always partially elsewhere. The expression on the face in front of him was different. It was the quality of someone running a rapid assessment of a situation and arriving at a conclusion.

"Can I help you?" she said.

The voice was correct. The cadence was correct. The slight upward inflection at the end of the question was consistent with the audio recordings in Winters's public lecture archive.

"I think I'm turned around," Kain said. "I'm looking for the research clinic."

"You're on the wrong floor." She pointed back the way he had come. "Main corridor, elevator to two, turn left."

"Thank you."

He turned and walked back toward the door. Behind him, her footsteps resumed  — not in the direction she had been heading, but back the way she had come. The assessment had concluded. The withdrawal protocol had activated.

He went through the door and stopped on the other side of it, his back against the wall, and counted to five.

Then he went back through.

The corridor was empty.

\*\*\*

The Bleed arrived without warning at the corner where the service passage turned toward the fire exit stairwell.

It did not arrive as a gap this time. It arrived as an addition — a layer of sensory data superimposed over the concrete corridor with a completeness that made the corridor itself seem like the intrusion. The baseline room. The room from the Sphere's calibration sequence, with its too-white walls and the specific quality of ozone that the entanglement field generated as a byproduct of the magnetic levitation system. The smell arrived first — sharp, chemical, the smell of air ionized by eleven megawatts of continuous electromagnetic output. Then the walls, pressing in from the periphery of his visual field, white over concrete, the two surfaces occupying the same space with equal insistence.

He stopped walking.

The concrete, conduit, and fluorescent tubes remained. The building's ventilation system hummed somewhere below the floor. But the baseline room existed with equal force, its ozone smell overriding the concrete dust and recycled air of the service passage, its white walls asserting themselves against the grey of the unpainted surface in front of him. The two environments did not alternate. They coexisted, each one fully present, each one demanding the full processing capacity of a nervous system that could not divide itself between them without losing coherence in both.

He pressed his right hand flat against the concrete wall.

The texture was specific — aggregate visible in the surface, the slight roughness of unfinished concrete against his palm, the cold of a material that had been absorbing the building's air conditioning for decades. He held the sensation and used it as an anchor. The baseline room's ozone smell did not disappear. It receded by degrees, the way a sound recedes when you move away from its source — still present, still identifiable, but no longer dominant.

The white walls pulled back to the periphery of his visual field.

Eleven seconds. He had lost eleven seconds.

He went around the corner.

The corridor beyond was empty. Thirty meters of straight concrete, two doors on the right side, a fire exit at the far end with a push bar and a small reinforced window. The push bar showed the same displacement he had learned to read — the mechanism not fully returned to its resting position, the left end sitting a fraction of a millimeter proud of the housing.

Someone had gone through it in the last sixty seconds.

He pushed through into the stairwell beyond.

\*\*\*

The stairwell connected to the hospital's external loading dock on the ground floor. He looked down through the gap between the stair flights and saw the ground floor landing lay empty; the door to the loading dock swung closed. 

He went down.

The loading dock was a covered concrete apron at the rear of the building, accessible from Commercial Road via a service lane. Two delivery vehicles parked against the far wall, their drivers absent. A hospital orderly sat on a loading bay step with a phone in his hand, not looking up. The service lane ran twenty meters to the street, and at the end of it, turning left onto Commercial Road, was the woman in the white coat.

She had removed the coat. She carried it folded over her left arm — the tablet gone, the ID badge gone, the white coat now a piece of clothing rather than a credential. The transformation had taken less than sixty seconds in the stairwell. The replacement had known the exit route before it entered the building.

Kain moved through the loading dock and into the service lane.

Commercial Road at nine-thirty on a Tuesday morning carried the specific density of a major arterial in a medical precinct — delivery vehicles, taxis, the slow accumulation of traffic around a hospital that drew its own sustained current of human movement. The footpath held the particular mix of people that gathered around large medical facilities: staff on breaks, visitors arriving and departing, the occasional patient moving with the careful deliberateness of someone recently discharged.

The woman was forty meters ahead, moving south at a pace that had changed. The correct, unhurried tempo of the corridor was gone. She moved now with the specific efficiency of someone executing a planned withdrawal — not running, not drawing attention, but covering ground at the maximum rate that did not constitute flight.

He followed.

The gap closed to thirty meters. Twenty-five. The footpath narrowed at a construction hoarding that extended from a building site across half the pedestrian space, compressing foot traffic into a single-file channel between the hoarding and the road. She entered the channel. He was ten seconds behind her.

The Bleed returned in the channel.

This time it was the laughing woman, and this time she was not behind his eyes. She occupied the space two meters ahead of him in the channel, her presence directional, located, as physically specific as the hoarding to his left and the road to his right. She laughed for four seconds — high, uncontrolled, the laugh of someone who had found something genuinely funny in a situation that contained nothing funny — and the sound had a position in space, a source point, as though she stood in the channel and was laughing at something she could see that he could not.

He stopped moving. The person behind him walked into his back and said something he did not process.
