The plywood was cheap, slightly damp from the morning's rain, the grain of the wood raised under his fingertips where the moisture had lifted it. He pressed his left hand flat against it and held the texture and did not move until the laughing woman stopped.

Four seconds.

He pushed through the remaining length of the channel and emerged onto the open footpath beyond, scanning south, north, the road, the opposite footpath.

Nothing.

She had used the Bleed. Not deliberately — she could not have known about the Bleed, could not have predicted the episode. But the timing had been exact, and the result was the same regardless of intent: fifteen seconds of combined operational blackout had given a person moving at her pace enough distance to disappear into the crowd.

He stood on the footpath in the rain and ran the inventory.

He had confirmed the replacement was operational. He had confirmed it used Winters's credentials and had a network contact inside the hospital — \*M. Reyes\*, a name written in a practiced hand on a blank intake form. He had confirmed it knew its exit routes and had a planned withdrawal protocol. He had confirmed it moved like someone reading a space rather than inhabiting it, and he had confirmed that the tablet orientation was a behavioral tell that would appear again.

He had not seen its face clearly. Twenty meters in the service corridor, her back to him for most of it. The face-to-face exchange had lasted three seconds at a distance of four meters, and his attention had been on the voice, the cadence, the assessment running behind her eyes. He had the bone structure. He had the coloring. He did not have enough.

He pulled out his phone and dialed Emily.

She picked up on the first ring.

"I lost it on Commercial Road," he said. "South of the loading dock. Planned withdrawal — coat off, credentials gone, exit route pre-mapped. It knew the building before it entered."

"How long did you have eyes on it?"

"Six minutes. Maybe seven."

"Did you get a visual?"

"Partial. Not enough." He started walking north, back toward the hospital. "The intake form in room two-fourteen has a name. M. Reyes. Run it against the QSA civilian contact registry and the Alfred patient database."

"That'll take twenty minutes."

"I'll be inside." He paused. "The ten-thirty data review session is still on the board. The replacement booked it. It may come back to complete it."

Silence on Emily's end. Not the silence of processing — the silence of someone deciding whether to say the thing they were about to say. "You think it'll return to a location it just fled."

"I think it has a network contact named Reyes and an unfinished data review session and a replacement protocol that requires both of those things to be completed before it moves to the next phase. Abandoning the session creates a gap in Winters's record that flags the breach. It won't want that gap."

"You're betting on its operational discipline."

"I'm betting on its instructions. There's a difference."

He ended the call and pushed back through the loading dock door.

\*\*\*

The atrium at ten-fifteen held a different population than the one at nine o'clock. The morning's appointment rush had passed through and dispersed into the building's upper floors, leaving behind the slower, more settled density of people who were waiting rather than arriving. The family near the information desk had rearranged themselves — the sleeping child now awake and eating something from a paper bag, the adults in the same configuration but with the particular quality of people who had stopped pretending the wait had a predictable end.

Kain took a different chair this time — one with a sightline to both the elevator bank and the main entrance, positioned behind a structural column that broke his silhouette without obstructing his view. He had twelve minutes before the ten-thirty session. He used four of them to review the photograph of the intake form on his phone, studying the handwriting in the name field. The letters were formed with the careful deliberateness of someone who had practiced the motion rather than developed it — the \*R\* in Reyes slightly too upright, the \*y\* descending at an angle that was geometrically correct but not natural.

The replacement had written the name. Not Winters. The replacement had created a patient record for its own network contact using a name it had been given rather than one it had generated, and it had written that name in a hand that was not Winters's hand because it had not yet had enough time in the building to absorb the fine motor residue of Winters's writing.

His phone buzzed. Emily.

"M. Reyes doesn't appear in the QSA civilian contact registry," she said. "The Alfred patient database has a Marcus Reyes, sixty-three, cardiac patient, last appointment fourteen months ago. No connection to the neurology research clinic."

"It's a cover name. The contact came in as a patient, used the intake form to pass something to the replacement, and left before the appointment officially ended."

"What did they pass?"

"I don't know yet." He kept his eyes on the elevator bank. "The data review session at ten-thirty. Is it still showing in the system?"

The pause lasted three seconds. "Yes. It's still active."

"Then the replacement is coming back."

At ten twenty-two, the elevator doors opened.

The woman who stepped out wore different clothing — dark trousers, a grey jacket, no white coat, no ID badge. Her hair was still pulled back. She carried nothing in either hand. She moved through the atrium toward the corridor that led to the research wing with the same correct, unhurried tempo she had used in the staff corridor, and her left hand swung at her side with the natural looseness of someone who was not carrying a tablet and had not yet remembered to adjust the behavioral pattern that the tablet had been anchoring.

Her left hand was still wrong. The fingers held a slight curl, the thumb angled outward — the residual posture of a hand that had been gripping a tablet for the last ninety minutes and had not yet released the muscle memory of it.

Kain stood up from the chair.

He followed her into the corridor at a distance of twenty meters, and the Bleed did not return, and his depth perception was off by an estimated eight percent, and the fluorescent light arrived at his eyes a fraction of a second after it should have, and none of that mattered because the replacement was twelve meters ahead of him and moving toward a room it had booked under a dead woman's credentials, and he had eleven minutes before the session was scheduled to begin, and he had not seen its face clearly, and he was not going to let that happen again.

He closed the distance to ten meters.

Eight.

The replacement reached the door to the research wing and badged through with Winters's keycard. The door swung closed behind it. He reached it four seconds later and used his Level 4 clearance to follow.

The corridor beyond was empty except for the replacement, now six meters ahead, moving toward room two-fourteen with the same correct tempo. He was close enough to hear her footsteps on the linoleum — a sound that was almost right, almost the sound of someone who had walked this corridor a thousand times, but not quite, the weight distribution fractionally off, the heel strike a degree too deliberate.

She reached room two-fourteen and stopped with her hand on the door.

And then she turned around.

She turned immediately and completely — not the cautious pivot of someone registering a noise, but the turn of someone who had known he was there for thirty seconds and had decided this was the moment to stop pretending otherwise.

He was five meters away. The corridor lighting was the same cold fluorescent that rendered every face the color of old bone. Her face was Winters's face — the bone structure, the coloring, the arrangement of features. But the expression was not Winters's expression, and it was not the rapid-assessment expression he had seen in the service corridor. It was something else. Something that had decided the withdrawal protocol was no longer the operative instruction.

She opened her mouth to speak.

The ozone arrived first.

Not the hospital's antiseptic-over-organic air. Not the managed institutional atmosphere of the research wing. Something else, arriving from inside the architecture of his own sensory processing rather than from the corridor's ventilation — the specific ionised sharpness that the Sphere's entanglement field produced as a byproduct of its electromagnetic output, a smell that had no business being on the second floor of Alfred Hospital and that his nervous system had catalogued across eleven deployments with the involuntary precision of a system that could not choose what to remember.

He had three seconds.

Not a prediction. The timing of the episodes had become, over four years of managed fragmentation, as readable as weather. The ozone was the pressure drop. The episode was the front. He had three seconds between the smell and the replacement.

He used one of them to take a step toward the wall.

He used the second to press his right hand flat against the plasterboard, the specific roughness of the surface under his palm, the cold of the building's air conditioning in the material.

He did not get to the third.  
Not the baseline room this time. Not the laughing woman. Something new — a complete sensory replacement, the corridor gone, the fluorescent light gone, the replacement's face gone, replaced by a darkness that was not the darkness of a closed room but the darkness of the Sphere's interior, the matte black surface two point eight meters in every direction, the subsonic hum of eleven megawatts moving through the platform below his feet, the ozone smell so concentrated it registered as a taste at the back of his throat.

He was inside the Sphere.

He was in the corridor.

Both were equally true and neither was navigable.

He pressed both hands against the wall to his right — the linoleum surface, the slight give of the plasterboard beneath it, the cold of the building's air conditioning in the material — and held the texture and counted his pulse and did not move.

When the Sphere's interior receded, he was on one knee in the corridor with no memory of going down.

The door to room two-fourteen was closed.

The corridor was empty.

He stayed on one knee for four seconds, running the inventory. Vision: degraded, peripheral field showing residual darkness from the Sphere's interior. Auditory: clear. Cognition: sequential reasoning present but slow, working memory showing significant gaps. Motor: gross motor compromised, fine motor unknown.

He stood up.

The door to room two-fourteen was locked. The keycard reader showed red. He used his Level 4 clearance and the reader cycled to green and he pushed through.

The room was empty. The desk surface was clear. The intake form with M. Reyes's name was gone.

He pulled out his phone and dialed Emily.

She picked up on the first ring.

"It's gone," he said. "And I still don't have the face."

The pause on Emily's end lasted two seconds. "Come back to the office."

"Not yet." He looked at the empty desk. "The intake form is gone. It took it. Which means whatever Reyes passed to it was on that form, and it didn't want to leave the paper trail."

"Kain." Her voice carried the specific quality of someone who had been deciding whether to say something for the last twenty minutes and had arrived at a decision. "Your last three transmissions have had a combined delay of eleven seconds. That's not a network issue."

He said nothing.

"Come back to the office," she said again. "Now."

He ended the call and stood in the empty room for a moment longer, and the Sphere's darkness did not return, and the laughing woman did not return, and the corridor outside was empty and fluorescent and exactly as long as it had always been, and he walked back through it toward the elevator with both hands at his sides and his depth perception eight percent off and the specific knowledge that the replacement had seen his face clearly, at a distance of five meters, in good light, for at least three seconds before the Bleed had taken him.

Which meant the network now knew he was here.

Which meant the geometry of everything had changed.

<a name="chapter-10"></a> [home](#home)

# **CHAPTER 10 — REYES**

The call came through a relay that added four seconds of latency and routed through three jurisdictions before it reached the phone in Reyes's coat pocket. Four seconds was the price of operational security. Reyes had been paying it for eleven years and had stopped noticing the pause the way people stopped noticing the sound of their own breathing.

The café occupied the ground floor of a building on Flinders Lane, narrow-fronted, the kind of establishment that had survived three cycles of Melbourne's hospitality economy by being exactly what it was without apology — dark wood, espresso machines that predated the quantum era's aesthetic preferences, a clientele that came for the coffee and stayed because the ambient noise level made conversation private without requiring effort. Reyes sat at the corner table with a long black and a newspaper that had not been read and answered on the second ring.

"The investigator made contact," the replacement said. Its voice carried the slight flatness of someone maintaining a vocal register they had learned rather than developed. Even through four seconds of relay latency, the quality was audible to someone who knew what to listen for. "Alfred Hospital, second floor research corridor. He used a Level 4 QSA field credential for the access points."

"Did he make the visual?"

"Partial. I terminated the engagement before he could complete it."

Reyes set the newspaper down. "How partial?"

"Three seconds at four meters. Corridor lighting. He was experiencing a neurological episode during the final approach — I observed the onset. His motor control degraded. He went to one knee."

The café's espresso machine produced a sound like a controlled detonation. Reyes waited for it to finish. "He went to one knee in the corridor."

"Yes."

"And you terminated the engagement."

"The withdrawal protocol was the operative instruction."

Reyes picked up the long black and held it without drinking. The ceramic was warm against the palm — a specific, grounding warmth, the kind that came from a cup that had been filled recently and had not yet begun to cool. "The withdrawal protocol applies to standard investigative contact. A dormant carrier running an active investigation with a documented neurological episode in the field is not standard investigative contact." A pause. "What was the episode?"

"Environmental superimposition. He pressed both hands against the wall and counted. Approximately fifteen seconds total duration. He recovered full motor function before I exited the room."

Fifteen seconds. Reyes ran the arithmetic without moving. A dormant carrier, activated by the Sphere's entanglement field, running an investigation under a 1.4x Bleed multiplier from sleep deprivation, experiencing environmental superimposition severe enough to produce motor failure. The medical literature on dormant carrier degradation was not widely available — the QSA had classified the relevant research within six months of the programme's first operational results. But Reyes had read the unclassified precursors, the papers that had been published before the classification order, and the arithmetic they contained was not complicated.

The carrier was approaching a threshold.

"What is his current operational status?" Reyes asked.

"Active. He returned to the atrium after the episode. He was on his phone — I assume coordinating with his handler. He re-entered the research wing at ten twenty-two."

"He came back."

"Yes."

Reyes set the cup down. The ceramic made a small sound against the wood of the table — precise, contained, the sound of something placed rather than dropped. "A dormant carrier experiencing motor failure from a Bleed episode, operating on thirty-plus hours without sleep, with a partial visual on a network asset, came back to the location where the asset had already made him." A pause. "What does that tell you?"

The replacement did not answer immediately. The four-second relay latency made the silence longer than it was. "That he prioritizes the investigation over his own physical integrity."

"It tells you he already knows he's terminal," Reyes said. "A man who believes he has time manages his resources. A man who knows he doesn't spends them." The espresso machine produced another controlled detonation. Reyes waited. "Who is his handler?"

"The credential was issued under QSA field operations. The handler identity is not in the accessible personnel registry."

"It's Emily Voss." Reyes said it the way people say things they have known for long enough that the knowing has become structural. "She's been running him since the Solis investigation. She has the journals."

Another pause — this one longer than the relay latency accounted for. "If Voss has the journals, she has the origin point of the replacement methodology."

"She has the journals and she hasn't used them," Reyes said. "Which means she's holding them for something. Which means she has her own operational agenda that is not identical to the carrier's agenda." The long black had cooled to the temperature at which coffee became drinkable rather than impressive. Reyes drank it. "This is the situation. You have a dormant carrier who is terminal and knows it, running an investigation he cannot complete before the Bleed takes him, being handled by an operator who is withholding the one piece of information that would allow him to complete it. Do you understand what that situation is?"

"An asset," the replacement said.

"An asset," Reyes agreed. "The network has been looking for a dormant carrier with active Sphere exposure for fourteen months. The QSA's programme produced eleven. Three survived. Two of the three survivors are non-functional. The third is in a care facility and has not spoken in four years." A pause. "This carrier is functional. He is degrading, but he is functional, and he is motivated, and he is already inside the investigation. The network does not need to recruit him. The network needs to point him."

"Point him at what?"

"At Voss." Reyes stood up from the table, leaving the newspaper and the empty cup. The café's ambient noise continued its work of making conversation private. "She has the journals. He doesn't know she has them — or he knows and hasn't moved on it yet, which amounts to the same operational problem. If the carrier discovers that his handler has been withholding the origin point of the replacement methodology, he will stop trusting her. If he stops trusting her, he stops being manageable. If he stops being manageable, he becomes unpredictable." A pause. "Unpredictable dormant carriers with active Sphere exposure and terminal Bleed progression are not useful to anyone."

"You want to tell him about the journals."

"I want him to find out about the journals in a way that feels like his own discovery." Reyes moved toward the café's door, unhurried, the coat buttoned against the morning's rain. "The intake form. The M. Reyes name. He photographed it."

"Yes."

"Good. That name connects to a QSA internal file that connects to Voss's operational history. The connection is not obvious — it requires three steps of cross-referencing that a Level 4 field credential can access but that a handler would not expect a field operative to pursue independently." A pause at the door. "He will pursue it independently. That is what terminal men do — they stop waiting for permission."

"And when he finds the connection to Voss?"

"He will confront her. She will make a decision about whether to show him the journals. If she shows him, the investigation accelerates and the network benefits from the carrier's remaining functional capacity. If she doesn't show him—" Reyes pushed through the door into the rain. "Then the carrier becomes unpredictable, and the network has a different kind of asset."

The call ended.

Reyes walked north on Flinders Lane in the rain, past the gallery frontages and the loading bays. He moved through the particular Melbourne architecture of laneways designed for service access that had become, over decades of incremental human decision-making, spaces people chose to inhabit.

The rain came down on the bluestone, the wet street reflecting the morning's grey light. Reyes walked through it with the unhurried efficiency of a man who had already made the only decision that mattered, simply moving through the time that followed it.

The carrier's name was Kain.

Reyes had known about him for six weeks — since the first anomaly in the B-734 investigation had flagged a dormant carrier with active Sphere exposure in the QSA's field operative registry. Six weeks of watching him work, watching the Bleed progress, watching him make the specific decisions that terminal men made: the decision to continue, the decision to withhold his own degradation from the people around him, the decision to go back to the location where he had already been made rather than retreat and reassess.

The network had not created Kain's situation. The network had simply recognized it for what it was.

A dormant carrier with active Sphere exposure, terminal Bleed progression, and a handler who was withholding the origin point of the replacement methodology was not a threat to the network. He was a mechanism. A mechanism that the network had not built and did not need to maintain and would not be responsible for when it reached the end of its operational life.

The rain intensified slightly. Reyes turned up the collar of the coat and kept walking.

The carrier had perhaps thirty jumps left before the Bleed became irreversible. Thirty jumps, or the equivalent neurological load from continued Sphere exposure without jumping — the medical literature was ambiguous on the precise conversion rate, but the order of magnitude was consistent. Thirty jumps. At the current pace of the investigation, that was enough.

Probably enough.

The word \*probably\* was not in Reyes's standard operational vocabulary.

Reyes ran probability assessments. Reyes ran risk gradients and resource calculations and the specific arithmetic of operational windows against degradation curves, and all of it produced numbers, not qualifications. Qualifications belonged to analysts who had not done the work. Reyes had done the work. The carrier had thirty jumps. Thirty jumps was enough at the current investigation pace. That was a number, not a probability.

He had written \*probably\* anyway.

He noted this without pursuing it, the way a person notes a sound they cannot account for and decides, after two seconds of listening, that it was nothing. The rain came down on the bluestone. The calculation was sound. The carrier would do what terminal men did: he would spend what he had, he would find what the network needed him to find, and he would reach the end of his operational life at the required moment.

Probably.

Reyes turned onto Swanston Street and disappeared into the morning crowd, and the rain came down on Melbourne, and somewhere in Alfred Hospital a man with thirty jumps left was looking at an empty room and deciding what to do next, and the network had already decided what it wanted him to do, and the distance between those two things was the operational space the network intended to occupy.

<a name="chapter-11"></a> [home](#home)

# **CHAPTER 11 **— BELL DISCOVERS THE LIE 

The first time Bell saw the failure, it was not in the field, where failures were loud and final. It was in the kitchen of the safe house, two weeks after the Alfred Hospital extraction, over a cup of black coffee that had gone cold.

Hayden was running a cross-reference on the Winters database query, his left hand braced against the edge of the counter, his right index finger tracking the data flow on the terminal with the speed and precision that she had once watched with detached professional admiration. He moved the cursor from a timeline to a geographical vector—a shift that required micro-musculature control—and the cursor twitched. Not a smooth, lateral move, but a small, involuntary jump, two pixels down and three to the left, before he corrected it.

It was negligible. The error was within the operating tolerance of a fatigued operator on a legacy terminal. He corrected it without comment, his gaze steady on the screen.

Bell’s hand was already moving. She did not reach for the tablet, did not make a sound. She reached for the kitchen clock and adjusted its digital time forward by forty-five seconds—an action so mundane and unnecessary that Hayden didn't look up, his attention locked on the data stream. She watched his neck, the line of his jaw. The correction of the cursor error had cost him a faint tightening of the masseter muscle, the same way a person tightens their jaw against the start of a deep, localized pain.

She filed the observation under a new category: ICS-Anomaly.

She walked around the counter, picked up her empty mug, and ran it under the tap, using the sound of the water to cover the two specific, purposeful movements she executed next. The first was to stop at the fridge, where she had clipped a laminated copy of their shared Fitzroy safe house floor plan to the magnetic surface. The second was to use the phone in her pocket—the secondary, compartmentalized device—to photograph the laminated plan.

She took the photograph not of the entire plan, but of a small, specific section: the corridor geometry between the main living area and the bedroom.

When she got back to the counter, Hayden had moved on. The cursor was tracking the data flow again, the movement steady, the tremor absent.

"Any luck on the Reyes alias?" she asked.

"Nothing clean. Just the old cardiac file. If they used a dead drop, it was paper and it's already gone." His voice was steady. "We shift to the temporal logs. Cross-reference the insertion windows against known QSA maintenance schedules."

"Done," she said.

She sat down at the table and opened the secondary device. She overlaid the photograph of the corridor geometry with a blank vector field. She then pulled the most recent movement logs from the safe house’s passive optical system—the logs they maintained for security redundancy, showing ingress and egress patterns for both of them.

She pulled Hayden's log for the last seven days: twenty-one movements through the main corridor. She compared them, one by one, against the overlaid geometry.

The corridor log documented his pace, his weight distribution, and his lateral drift—the minute-by-minute, sub-conscious negotiation of his body with the narrow space. For the first nineteen entries, the log was clean: his body favored the left side of the corridor, his pace was consistent, his weight distribution symmetrical.

The twentieth entry—the one logged four hours after the cursor failure—showed a difference.

The pace was identical. The weight distribution was symmetrical. But the lateral drift was wrong. On the return trip from the bedroom to the living area, his body drifted six centimetres to the right, bumping the side table that sat against the wall. The log registered the impact as a single, high-frequency spike in the acoustic record, a sound she had interpreted at the time as a book being moved.

She looked at the acoustic data again. It was not a book. It was the soft thud of a hip bone striking wood.

Six centimetres. The kind of deviation a person made when they were walking in the dark, or when they were walking with a cognitive disconnect between intention and execution. Hayden never walked in the dark.

She filed the anomaly under ICS-Anomaly (Proprioceptive).

She then cross-referenced the lateral drift event with the clock. 04:17.

That time had been accounted for. She had looked up when he entered the living area, asking if he needed anything, and he’d said no, just a comm check. She had filed it under routine and gone back to sleep.

She looked at the anomaly. Six centimetres. A tremor. A bump that was not a bump. Three facts, each one individually explicable, each one pointing in the same direction. She had been observing him for weeks, and she had filed every instance under the assumption that his body was failing.

She had failed to consider the second explanation: that his body was failing, and he was deliberately concealing the failure from her.

She closed the file. The logical path from 'he has a tremor' to 'he is lying' was not a single step. It was a climb. And she was now at the base of the mountain, looking up at the summit she had deliberately avoided looking at for four years.

She opened the private partition and navigated to the QSA archive access log. The log that contained every document Hayden had ever accessed using his full Level 5 clearance—the archive she had maintained for operational redundancy, not for auditing. The archive she had never used.

She typed the query: ICS Log \- J. Hayden \- Full. Decrypt.

The screen populated. She got as far as column six before she stopped.

ICS post-jump, jump 1: 96\.

She cross-referenced it against the QSA baseline documentation she'd lifted from Hayden's operational archive eight months ago, when the tremor in his left hand started and he told her it was a nerve compression from the Southbank extraction. She'd believed him. She'd watched him ice the hand and filed it under physical maintenance and said nothing more about it, and let it go because she was a good partner and good partners did not audit each other's medical presentations.

She had been, she now understood, auditng everything except the thing that mattered.

ICS post-jump, jump 100: 71\.

She kept reading. 

The anomaly was in the timestamps.

That was what Bell had been tracking for six weeks, in the private partition she'd carved off the secondary comm device — the one Hayden thought she used for legacy network contacts, dead-drop scheduling, the administrative sediment of fieldwork. She did use it for those things. She also used it for this: a quiet, accumulating file of discrepancies she had not yet decided what to do with.

She was a good operational planner. She understood that the decision of what to do with a thing could wait until the shape of the thing was fully known. Premature conclusions were how people got killed on jobs. So she had been patient. She had built the file the way she built extraction routes — methodically, without attachment to any particular outcome, testing each data point against the adjacent ones until the geometry either resolved or it didn't.

For six weeks, it hadn't resolved. The timestamps kept failing the test.

The first one had been the Alfred entry log — a routine cross-reference she'd run against their safe house sequencing to verify the B-734 insertion window. Standard practice. She ran it the same way she ran everything, because good logistics required a complete picture and a complete picture required checking the pieces you already trusted as well as the ones you didn't. The Alfred entry log showed Hayden badging in at 02:17. The safe house departure log, which she had written herself, showed him leaving at 02:31. Fourteen minutes. The corridor from the safe house to the Alfred insertion point ran six minutes at a controlled pace. She had walked it herself in the third week, clocking the variables — foot traffic, door weight, the slight uphill grade on Punt Road that added twenty seconds in wet conditions.

Fourteen minutes left four unaccounted for.

She had filed it under *safe house exit delay* and moved on. There were three dozen variables in any insertion that could absorb four minutes — a comm check that ran long, a biometric rescan at the first secure door, a recalibration if the field readings were outside tolerance. She knew this. She also knew that Hayden logged every delay in the prep notes, and the prep notes for that insertion contained no delay notation.

She had photographed the prep notes and added them to the partition file.

That was the first one. The subsequent five weeks had produced eleven more — each one individually explicable, each one sitting within the range of normal operational variance, each one pointing in the same direction when you laid them end to end on a table and looked at the geometry from above.

Hayden was losing time. Not in the field — in transit, in the intervals between logged events, in the negative space of a schedule she had been reading without understanding what she was reading.

The Alfred Hospital prep file was the first one she'd pulled with full decryption access. Hayden managed the QSA-facing documentation; she managed the physical logistics, the safe house sequencing, the comm protocols. Their division of labour was clean and had been clean for four years. It had never occurred to her to look at the jump records directly. She'd seen the summaries. She'd seen what he showed her, which she now understood was not the same thing.

The decryption took forty seconds. The file populated in columns: jump number, date, target reality, duration, ICS pre-jump, ICS post-jump, recovery window, clinical notes.

She got as far as column six before she stopped.

ICS post-jump, jump 1: 96\.

She cross-referenced it against the QSA baseline documentation she'd lifted from Hayden's operational archive eight months ago, when the tremor in his left hand started and he told her it was a nerve compression from the Southbank extraction. She'd believed him. She'd watched him ice the hand and filed it under *physical maintenance* and said nothing more about it, and let it go because she was a good partner and good partners did not audit each other's medical presentations.

She had been, she now understood, auditng everything except the thing that mattered.

ICS post-jump, jump 100: 71\.

She kept reading.

The clinical notes began at jump 47 — brief, technical, the kind of shorthand that read like log entries from a mechanic rather than a man. *Residue latency increased 0.3 seconds. No functional impairment.* At jump 67: *Post-transition proprioceptive adjustment window extended to 140 seconds. Within acceptable parameters. Unreported.* At jump 89: *Reflection lag present, approximately 0.5 seconds in fluorescent environments. Unreported to QSA medical.* At jump 112: *Balance disruption, four seconds, left-side bias. Concurrent memory insertion, female subject, unknown origin. Duration: eleven seconds. Unreported.*

She stopped at jump 112\. Went back. Read it again.

*Female subject, unknown origin.*

The way he'd described the Bleed in the early months, returned to her now. When she'd asked him directly — sitting in the kitchen of the first safe house, the one in Fitzroy with the draughty sash windows, over coffee that had gone cold because they'd been talking too long. He'd said: *fragments, mostly. Environmental — a room you've been in, a smell, a sound. Nothing coherent. Nothing that stays.*

She had believed that too.

*Unreported.* The word appeared eleven times in the first hundred and fifty entries. She counted them. She was the kind of person who counted things rather than estimating them, because estimates were how errors accumulated and accumulated errors were how operations failed. Eleven times. Eleven instances of information that had been generated, documented in his own hand, and withheld from the QSA medical division that was supposed to be monitoring his neurological integrity.

Withheld from her.

ICS post-jump, jump 200: 58\.

Bell's left hand rested flat on the table. She noted it without naming it. She didn't move it.

Jump 200's clinical notes ran longer than the others. The handwriting had changed — she was reading a physical scan of handwritten entries, and up to jump 199 the script was compressed, efficient, the same hand that annotated extraction routes and circled abort triggers and wrote corridor timings in the margins of floor plans. At jump 200 it loosened at the margins. Not dramatically. The letters were still formed correctly, still legible, still recognisably his. But the pressure was different — the pen moving faster, the ink pooling fractionally at the end of each stroke as though the hand had committed to a speed it couldn't quite sustain. The handwriting of a man writing faster than he wanted to think.

*Memory insertion duration increasing. Fourteen seconds, consistent female subject, unknown apartment, winter light. Subject laughs at something I can't hear. ICS calculation: current trajectory projects threshold breach at approximately jump 340\. Adjusted projection assumes continued anchor proximity. Anchor variable reduces bleed rate by estimated 23%. Departure of anchor variable would accelerate cascade. Current operational posture: maintain anchor proximity. Prioritise.*

She read it twice.

The third time she reached *anchor variable* the comm board on the near wall pulsed twice — soft amber, their check-in frequency. She had seventeen minutes left in her audit window. She had estimated twenty-five minutes for the full log. The check-in was three minutes early.

She looked up.

The Docklands extraction route was pinned to the ops board at eye level, laminated, the lines of her own geometry running clean and deliberate across the grid. She'd drafted it on a Tuesday in March, the week before the job, using the warehouse table and a physical stylus because some work needed to be done by hand to be done correctly. Three egress vectors, two abort triggers, four timing windows colour-coded by risk gradient. Hayden had sat across from her with a red marker and said *what about the tram corridor* and she'd said *no, it's too variable after nine PM* and he'd looked at the route for a long moment and then made two small corrections in the upper quadrant — shaved thirty seconds off the secondary abort, opened a third-choice corridor she hadn't considered — and said *just in case*.

His handwriting in the upper quadrant was present-tense. Functional. The handwriting of a man who trusted his own judgment and wanted her to trust it too.

The comm pulsed again.

Bell set the secondary device face-down on the table. She kept her eyes on the Docklands route. On the red annotations. On the *just in case* corridor in the upper quadrant that had, in fact, been the one they used when the primary abort failed at 21:47 and she'd said *secondary* and he'd already said *third* and they'd moved through it together without breaking stride, because they were good at this, because four years of shared fieldwork built something that worked even when it shouldn't, even in the dark, even when the geometry was bad and the timing was worse and the only thing holding the operation together was the fact that they had both been paying attention.

She picked up the comm device.

She said: "Confirmed."

From the bedroom — from wherever he was sitting with his back to the door the way he always sat, facing the window even when the window showed nothing, the habit of a man who had spent too many years needing to know what was outside: "Holding."

The comm closed.

She held the Docklands route in her eyeline for the full thirty seconds their protocol required. His red ink in the upper quadrant. The *just in case* corridor. The two corrections that had saved the job. She looked at them and she did not look away, because the Docklands route was the last thing in the room that existed in the register she had understood it to exist in twenty minutes ago, and she understood, with the part of her that processed operational logistics before it processed anything else, that the moment she looked away from it the room would never be the same configuration again.

Thirty seconds.

She put the comm device down and turned the secondary device face-up and kept reading.

ICS post-jump, jump 300: 49\.

Clinical note, jump 300: *Severe erosion threshold breached. Functional impairment: intermittent. Proprioceptive confusion, 3-8 seconds per episode, frequency increasing. Tremor present and persistent in left hand. Pharmacological protocol initiated. Neuroinhibitor dosing: prophylactic, daily. Procurement: independent, untraceable. Anchor variable still present. Bleed rate: managed. Recommend no operational changes. Recommend continued non-disclosure to anchor variable.*

She thought about the fourteen-minute gap in the Alfred entry log. The four unaccounted minutes. She had walked the corridor herself and clocked the variables and filed the discrepancy under *safe house exit delay* because there were always variables in an insertion and she had trusted his prep notes.

The prep notes had not mentioned a balance disruption. Had not mentioned proprioceptive confusion. Had not mentioned that his legs had been unreliable for six months and that he had been managing it alone, without telling her, because telling her would trigger departure and departure would accelerate cascade and he had run the calculation six times and decided the cost of disclosure was too high.

She kept reading.

Jump 340's entry changed the character of the handwriting permanently. She'd been watching for it since jump 200\. She found it in the upper-right notation of a page otherwise identical to the preceding ones — a small, corrective bracket around the phrase *cognitive integrity* that suggested he'd written it, stopped, considered striking it, and decided to leave it. The bracket was tight, precise, a habit of self-correction she had seen in every floor plan he'd ever annotated. He had written the phrase, doubted it, and chosen honesty over revision.

Below the bracket, in smaller script: *ICS 41\. The man who started this log would not recognise the calculus I am currently running. I find I don't have an opinion about that.*

She sat with that sentence for longer than she sat with any of the others.

The man who started this log. As though he were filing a report on someone he had known well and had since lost contact with. As though the distance between jump 1 and jump 340 were not a timeline but a translation — one language becoming another, the original no longer recoverable without a key he no longer possessed.

*I find I don't have an opinion about that.*

That was the line that told her everything the clinical shorthand hadn't. Not the ICS numbers, not the bleed multipliers, not the pharmacological procurement protocol. The absence of opinion. A man who had documented his own dissolution across three hundred and forty jumps and had arrived, at the far end of it, at a place where the dissolution no longer registered as loss.

Jump 380: ICS 34\. The clinical notes at jump 380 occupied a full page. She read them slowly, at the pace she read floor plans when the stakes were high — every word, every notation, nothing skimmed.

*Continuous tremor now bilateral. Balance disruption frequency: daily. Memory insertion duration: up to 47 seconds. Insertions now include sensory data — temperature, scent, ambient sound. Female subject consistent across all insertions. Apartment consistent. Winter light. She laughs at something. I still can't hear it.*

*ICS trajectory: terminal within 60-90 jumps absent intervention. Pharmacological management extending window. Anchor proximity: still primary mitigation variable. I have run the calculation. Without anchor proximity, current trajectory projects full cascade within 14-20 jumps. With anchor proximity, window extends to 40-55 jumps. Difference: 21-35 jumps.*

*I require the window. The mission requires the window. Continued non-disclosure assessed as operationally necessary. Anchor variable's awareness of clinical status would trigger departure and accelerate cascade. Assessment: the cost of disclosure outweighs the cost of non-disclosure.*

The calculus was clean. It was a perfectly executed piece of institutional logic, and it was devastating not because it was wrong, but because it was accurate. She ran the numbers again: 21-35 jumps. That was the window she provided. Every time she sat in the chair across from him, every time she made coffee in the morning, every time she looked at him and said nothing about the tremor, she was performing an operational function. She was a physical boundary, a neurological shield, a necessary component—not a partner. Hayden had spent four years defining their relationship in clinical terms, calculating the cost of her awareness against the cost of his life. The professional cruelty of it was a pressure on her sternum, a feeling of mass that did not belong there. He had not lied about the job; he had lied about the physics of their connection. He had chosen the quantifiable asset over the unquantifiable human, and the log was the proof that the choice had been made, documented, and filed without a single internal expression of regret. He hadn’t simply kept a secret; he had made her one. She was the one who had run the logistics, who had set the safe house grid, who had believed their four years together represented a shared commitment, and all of it, every minute, had been an equation running toward a single, clinical truth: Bell’s utility was maximized by her ignorance. She closed her eyes. The thought was sharp: he had traded her integrity for thirty-five jumps, and he had slept well every night knowing the transaction was complete.  

He had run it six times. It was accurate.

Bell set the device flat on the table and looked at the wall above the ops board. The blank section of wall to the left of the Docklands route where she'd taken down the safe house grid last week when the third location went dark. The rectangle of slightly less faded paint where it had hung. She had taken it down because the safe house was no longer relevant, and she had replaced it with nothing because there had been nothing ready to replace it with, and she had looked at that rectangle of paler paint every day since and not registered it as anything except a scheduling gap.

She registered it now as space she would need to move through on her way out.

She picked the device back up.

Jump 417\. Three days ago.

ICS post-jump: 32\.

The clinical note at jump 417 was four words.

*I can't hear her.*

She set the secondary device face-down on the table for the last time.  

Four words. Jump 417\. I can't hear her. The clinical note that was not clinical — the one place in four hundred and seventeen entries where the log had cracked open and let something through that was not notation. She did not know if he had meant it as a confession or simply as a record of the thing that was happening, and she had decided, in the thirty seconds she spent with those four words, that the distinction did not change anything. He had written it down. The writing was the record. The record was what she had.  
She stood up from the table.  

She did not pack the way she had packed on operational timelines — fast, decisive, the minimum necessary. She packed the way she did everything now: with the specific, considered deliberateness of someone for whom each action was final. The go-bag first, because the go-bag was already packed and already hers, containing nothing she had acquired in this flat, nothing that had come from the shared geography of the last four years. Then three items from the shelf in the bedroom: the navigation drive, the spare comms device in its case, and the small framed photograph she had carried through every safe house since 2151 and which she would not leave for the same reason she would not leave any piece of her operational infrastructure in an unsecured location.  

She did not take the Docklands route from the ops board.  

She stood in the bedroom doorway and looked at it on the wall — the floor plan under the acetate, the red vectors, his handwriting in the upper quadrant. Just in case. She had looked at it every day for three months. She would not take it because it was not hers to take. It was the record of an operation they had run together, and the operation had been real, and she was leaving the record where it belonged.  

She crossed the living room.  

Hayden was at the kitchen table. He had not moved in the hour she had been reading. He sat in the position she had registered when she first looked up from the device at the table's start — right hand flat on the surface, left hand in his lap, his face toward the window that showed the scaffolding and the winter dark behind it. He did not turn when she entered. She had not expected him to. He had known, she understood now, that the log existed. He had known she would find it eventually. That was what anchor variable meant — a variable whose behaviour he had modelled and projected and found acceptable within the parameters of the mission window. Her discovery was not a surprise. It was a scheduled event.  

She stopped at the table.  

She did not say: you used me. She did not say: I would have stayed if you'd asked. Both were true and neither required saying; he had access to both conclusions and had run them and had decided, six times, that the cost of disclosure outweighed the cost of non-disclosure, and the log was the proof that the decision had been made, and documented, and filed, and she had nothing to add to a decision that had been made with more thoroughness than most decisions she had ever made herself.  

What she said was: "Your ICS."  

The window held the scaffolding and the dark.  

"32," he said.  

She had read jump 417 three minutes ago. She knew it was 32\. She had asked because she needed to hear him say it in a voice that was his voice, in a room that was a real room, with a number that was the real number rather than a notation in a file. He had said it the way he said all numbers — without affect, the same way he had said forty-eight seconds and 21:47 and three hundred and forty kilograms per square metre. The voice did not crack on it. The voice was the voice she had been listening to for four years and it said 32 the way it said everything: as a fact that had been assessed and was now being reported.  

"I know," she said.  

She picked up the go-bag. She moved to the door.  

She stopped with her hand on the handle and she did not look back at him, because she had looked at him across every table in every safe house for four years and she had the record of it and she did not need the record extended. What she looked at was the door — the handle under her palm, the surface of it, the specific temperature of metal in a room that was cold but not as cold as the night would be.  

"The third vector," she said. "In the upper quadrant."  

A pause. "I know."  

"At 21:47."  

"I know."  

She opened the door and went out.  

The stairs held the particular cold of a building whose heating had been calibrated for occupancy rather than for the spaces between occupied areas. She descended them with her hand on the rail, her grip firm rather than necessary — the force of someone keeping contact with a fixed surface not because the surface was required but because the surface was there. The street door opened onto Fitzroy in the middle of the night, and the night was cold and contained the distant sound of Commercial Road traffic and the specific ambient quality of a city operating at reduced hours, and she stood on the footpath for a moment and oriented herself in it.  

She had a contact in Collingwood who owed her three favours, the first of which she had been saving for a situation that required a clean accommodation trail. She would collect it tonight. In the morning she would begin the process of making herself difficult to find, which was a process she had already run twice in four years and which she knew the steps of.  

She did not look up at the kitchen window.  

She walked.  

In the flat, Hayden sat at the table with his right hand on the surface and his left hand in his lap and the ops board on the wall with the Docklands route still pinned above the paler rectangle of paint where the safe house grid had been. The scaffolding on the building opposite held its night configuration. The green mug — chipped at the rim, carried through three safe houses — sat on the table where he had set it.  

He was aware, with the precision that the clinical notes did not have a notation for, that his pharmacological floor had just been removed.  

He sat with the arithmetic for a long time.  

Then he picked up the device and opened the jump log and made the entry for the day in the small, compressed handwriting of someone who had always written faster than they wanted to think.  

Bell is gone, he wrote. ICS 32\. Window: 14-20 jumps without anchor. The calculation was accurate. The cost was accurate. I find I don't have an opinion about that either.  

He set the device down.  

Outside, the scaffolding threw its shadow across the kitchen floor and moved by small degrees as the night continued its ordinary work.

---

She closed the file.

She sat for a moment with her hands flat on the table — both of them now, symmetrical, the way she positioned herself before a job when she needed her nervous system to understand that the planning phase was over and execution had begun.

The receipt was on the kitchen bench. She'd found it in his jacket pocket six weeks ago when she'd been looking for the building access card he'd misplaced — which was itself a symptom she'd filed under *fatigue* before she had a better category for it. A pharmacy receipt, not their usual procurement channel, itemised with a generic brand she'd had to look up. Prophylactic neuroinhibitors, daily dosing, three-month supply. She'd photographed it and replaced it and said nothing, because the shape of the thing was not yet fully known.

The shape of the thing was now fully known.

She stood, went to the kitchen, picked up the receipt, and placed it in the partition file along with everything else she had collected. Timestamps, the pharmacy receipt, the ICS trajectory she had now reconstructed across 417 data points. The file was thorough. It was the kind of file a person built when they understood, on some level, that they were going to need it to be thorough.

She did not call Emily.

She had thought, in the abstract, that Emily would be the correct person to contact. Emily had resources, institutional reach, the ability to pull Hayden off the field before jump 418\. She had also, at some point in the last four years, approved the operational parameters that allowed a man with an ICS of 41 to continue active fieldwork without mandatory disclosure to his partner. Bell had no direct evidence of that. She had sufficient inference. The QSA did not run operations like this without authorisation at the handling level, and Emily was the handler, and the handler knew the asset's status or the handler was negligent, and Emily was not negligent.

She did not call Kain. She did not know Kain. She knew his name from Hayden's early-cycle logs — the QSA Norm consultant, the dormant carrier Emily had brought in as a procedural instrument. She knew Kain the way she knew other moving parts in an operation she hadn't planned: as a variable to account for, not to contact. Whatever Kain was walking into in next, he would walk into it without knowing the anchor variable was already in motion. That was not her problem to solve. She had one operation running now, and it required her complete attention.

She went to the bathroom. Ran cold water. Looked at her own hands under the tap — both of them steady, which was information. Her nervous system had already accepted the transition from *planning* to *execution* and was running accordingly. She turned the tap off.

In the bedroom, Hayden moved — chair scraping slightly, the particular rhythm of someone shifting weight without standing up. She knew the acoustics of the apartment the way she knew the acoustics of every space she'd worked in for more than a month. She knew his weight distribution on the desk chair versus the floor. She knew the difference between the scrape that meant he was stretching and the scrape that meant he was standing, and this was the first one, which meant she had time.

She went back to the main room.

She pulled up the secondary comm device and navigated to the Docklands extraction route file — the original, not the laminated copy on the board. She annotated it with the timestamp of tonight's check-in, added it to the partition file, and closed the partition.

Then she unpinned the laminated route from the ops board.

She stood with it for a moment. His handwriting in the upper quadrant. The *just in case* corridor. The red marker annotations that had saved the Docklands job and that she had looked at every day for six weeks while building a file that now explained exactly what kind of instrument she had been to the man who made them. She rolled the laminate carefully — she was always careful with physical materials, this had not changed and would not change — and placed it in the flat sleeve she used for documents that needed to travel without damage.

She put the sleeve in her go-bag.

She did not take anything else that night. That was the point. One item, chosen for operational relevance, removed without altering the visual register of the room. She had thirty-one minutes of declared network maintenance remaining. She used nine of them.

When Hayden came out of the bedroom thirty-five minutes later and found her at the comm board running what looked like standard legacy contact management, she had already decided on the sequence. Not when. She hadn't decided when. She had decided the geometry: incremental, untriggered, each removal calibrated below the threshold of his residue-reading. The go-bag had always been partially packed. The sleeve had always been a document sleeve in a bag that had always contained document sleeves. The ops board had gaps from prior safe houses going dark. One more gap read as operational maintenance.

She understood residue-reading because he had taught her. He had sat across from her in the early months — the Fitzroy safe house, the draughty windows, the coffee going cold — and explained how a replacement betrayed itself. Not through facts, but through the absence of the psychological logic behind facts. The *why* of a person's habits. He had said: *the human animal leaves a pattern in a room, and the pattern is not the furniture, it's the friction between the furniture and the person's body. You learn to read the friction.*

She had learned to read the friction.

He had been teaching her a skill that was also, in some sense, a confession of method. That the man who could read the friction of a stranger's apartment had spent four years producing, for her benefit, a perfectly calibrated friction. The right habits in the right register. The red annotations. The *just in case* corridor. The thirty-second check-in delivered in the same tone whether the preceding fourteen minutes had been routine network maintenance or the discovery that she was a pharmacological instrument with a documented replacement cost of twenty-three percent.

Twenty-three percent.

That was what she was worth, expressed in the language he used when he thought no one was reading.

She said nothing. She ran her legacy contact maintenance. She went to bed when he went to bed. She lay in the dark and listened to him breathe — the slightly irregular rhythm that had developed around jump 300, the one she'd filed under *fatigue* and then under *nerve compression* and then under the growing category of things she had accepted his explanation for because she had been paying attention to everything except the right thing. The rhythm was present-tense. It was the breathing of a man still running, still operational, still convinced the window was sufficient. Still convinced she didn't know.

She was very still and very awake until well past three in the morning.

By then she had the geometry fully mapped.

She would leave in increments small enough that he would not clock the decay until she was already gone. Not for his sake. For hers — because a clean exit was a professional exit, and she was, regardless of what the last four years had been in his clinical shorthand, a professional. She would not slam a door. She would not create a confrontation that gave him information about the timeline of her departure. She would remove herself from the equation the same way she removed herself from any compromised operational environment: quietly, completely, and without leaving a retrievable pattern.

And because if he knew she was leaving before she was gone, he would still have the window. Twenty-three percent. Thirty-five jumps.

She was not willing to be the thing that gave him those jumps.

She closed her eyes. She did not sleep for a long time.

In the morning she made coffee — black, the way she'd always made it, the way he had presumably documented that she made it in a log entry she hadn't reached before the check-in cut her window short. She would read the remaining entries later, in the partition file, on the secondary device, somewhere he was not.

She handed him a cup. He said *thanks* in the distracted register of someone already inside their operational thinking, his attention on the terminal and not on her, which was operationally convenient and not, at this point, anything else. She said nothing.

His left hand tremored slightly around the mug. He clamped it with his right without looking at it. The correction was smooth and practiced — a compensation so thoroughly integrated into his physical routine that it no longer registered as a correction, just as a way of holding a cup. It was entirely invisible unless you had been watching it for six weeks and knew exactly what you were seeing and had now read, in his own clinical shorthand, the entry where he had first documented it and decided not to tell her.

She looked at her own coffee.

Outside, Melbourne ran its morning traffic through the gap between the warehouse roller door and the sill — trams, early freight, the particular acoustic texture of a city resuming itself after rain. She had been here long enough to know this sound the way she knew his breathing. Long enough that leaving would mean re-learning a different city's acoustics from scratch, in a safe house that didn't have his chair scrape or his three-AM breathing or the slight drag of his right boot heel on the concrete floor that she had clocked on day four and filed under *gait* and never thought about again.

She drank her coffee.

She did not look at the ops board, where the laminated route was no longer pinned.

He hadn't noticed yet.

<a name="chapter-12"></a> [home](#home)

# **CHAPTER 12 — THE TUESDAY CONTACT**

The laptop ran hot on the cheap aluminum table.

It was 4:30 AM in the Fitzroy North temporary office, a room rented with cash and configured with exactly two pieces of QSA-adjacent technology: the router, which was routed through three jurisdictions Kain had prepaid using untraceable crypto, and the laptop, which ran a Level 4 field credential. The rest of the equipment was civilian-grade—a thermal printer, a second monitor, and the two coffee mugs that held the last of the instant caffeine he had found in the cupboard. He had a two-hour window before the local council’s automated sweep detected the router’s operational signature and flagged the IP block for a manual review. Two hours to complete the third step of the cross-reference.

The M. Reyes alias had provided the necessary first step. Reyes, M. — cardiac patient, sixty-three, last appointment fourteen months ago. The name was a cover, but covers required construction, and construction required source material. Kain was looking for the seam where the cover story connected to the QSA’s institutional architecture.

He ran the query on a non-QSA terminal, routing through a secure proxy server he had maintained since the Varn extraction, using his Level 4 credential to pull the QSA civilian contact registry. The search parameters were narrow: M. Reyes, all operational liaisons between the QSA’s Civilian Interface Division (CID) and the Alfred Hospital system over the last five years.

The first pass yielded nothing. Reyes was a civilian. His cardiac file was clean. The link to the neurology research clinic, which the replacement had used to pass the note, was non-existent. The connection was buried deeper than the name.

Kain reset the query. He ran the second step: all CID operational files between the dates of January 2153 and June 2154—the fourteen-month window preceding Chen’s replacement—cross-referenced against the Alfred Hospital’s staff movement logs. He was looking for a pattern of anomaly: a QSA operative who had been near the hospital at the wrong time, or whose documented activity failed to match the clinical reality.

The query returned 38,000 entries. Too large. He needed a pin.

He took the folded paper from his jacket pocket—the one the target had left on the tram. He unfolded it now. It was blank. The target had left him nothing but the name, the warning, and the confidence that he would do the rest.

Kain crumpled the paper and threw it into the wastebasket. The confidence was the message.

He returned to the terminal and reframed the problem using the residue theory. The network was efficient. They didn't invent components; they repurposed them. If the Reyes alias was a functional cover, it had been drawn from a QSA asset who was already connected to the M. Reyes cardiac file.

He ran a new search: Alfred Hospital Staff, all departments, cross-referenced against Civilian Interface Division active and suspended personnel, all common surnames.

The query narrowed the list to 412 names. Still too large.

He needed a logical gap. He returned to the cardiac file. Marcus Reyes, age 63\. Two appointments over five years. Annual check-ups. The file was dull, boring, and medically inert, which was the point. The only deviation was a single insurance claim for a missing personal item filed in May 2154, eight months before Chen’s replacement. A watch. Claim denied.

Kain had read 38,000 files in the last two hours. The watch was the only thing that had been lost.

He cross-referenced the denial of the watch claim with the QSA’s internal financial management system. QSA operatives were prohibited from claiming personal losses sustained during operational duties through civilian insurance. The operative’s personal loss was absorbed by the QSA’s Operational Reserve Fund. The denial of the claim was the administrative process working correctly.

But Marcus Reyes was not an operative. He was a civilian cardiac patient.

The only reason a civilian’s claim would be denied under QSA protocols was if the item was lost by a QSA operative who had been using the civilian’s credentials.

He opened the Operational Reserve Fund ledger. The claim was listed: Loss of Personal Timepiece \- M. Reyes \- Operational Denial \- Ref: OPS-CONV-784-C.

The reference number was the pin.

He ran the final query: OPS-CONV-784-C – Full Operational History.  

The result populated the screen with a single, clear file:

* Designation: Operative Delta-6 (Voss, E. \- Civilian Interface Division)  
* Operational Context: Acquisition and sequestration of compromised field asset (Solis, M. – Threshold Program Director)  
* Asset Used: Marcus Reyes (Civilian Identity, Cardiac Cohort)  
* Summary: Operative Delta-6 utilized Reyes’s civilian identity for a three-hour window to access the Alfred Hospital’s off-network archive. Loss of personal timepiece (wrist chronometer, QSA issue, traceable via GPS tag) sustained during a logistical error in the extraction corridor. Operative Delta-6 filed the claim under Reyes’s civilian insurance to cover the loss of the watch, which was denied per Section 3A, Paragraph 4, Sub-clause ii. The item was subsequently recovered.  
* Note: The Reyes alias is now flagged for internal QSA audit. Operational use prohibited pending review.

Kain set his coffee mug down.

The target on the tram—the one who had his face, who had an ICS of 32, who had moved with the unsteady gait of a failing man—had passed a note through an alias that Emily had personally used to betray the Threshold Program's director, Martina Solis, four years ago. The QSA had flagged the Reyes alias as compromised and prohibited its use. The replacement network had used it anyway, in the one location—Alfred Hospital—that connected Kain's investigation to Emily's history.

The message was complete. The network was not just chasing Emily. It was using her own institutional crimes to point Kain directly at her.

He looked at the digital clock on the laptop screen. 6:28 AM. The time was gone. The two-hour window for the municipal sweep was over. The router's operational light was still green. He had run the audit in a blind spot.

He closed the terminal, pulled the encryption key, and placed the laptop in the travel bag. He had the name of the file—Solis, M. – Threshold Program Director—and the knowledge that Emily's operational history was tied directly to the Reyes alias. The tram was his next move.

He needed to run the confrontation on the tram with the precision the QSA expected of a man who had not slept in two days, who had a terminal diagnosis, and who had just discovered his handler was running a conspiracy inside a conspiracy. 

\*\*\* 

The 96 tram ran on schedule.

That was the first anomaly.

Kain had spent eleven days on this route — Bourke Street to Nicholson, the long diagonal cut through the city's eastern grid — and the 96 ran on schedule perhaps twice in a working week. Today it arrived at the Bourke Street stop at 7:43 AM, which was the published time, which meant the platform cameras at the Bourke Street Mall interchange had a clean, unobstructed sightline to every boarding passenger for the full forty seconds the doors stood open. No crowd surge. No umbrella obstruction. No delivery vehicle blocking the kerb lane.

The QSA surveillance array was not subtle about what it was. Three cameras per stop, mounted at the standard VicTrack height of 4.2 metres, each unit housing a dual-lens configuration — one optical, one thermal — with a refresh rate that the QSA's technical division had confirmed could resolve a face at twelve metres in 0.3 seconds. The array did not belong to VicTrack. VicTrack's cameras were the older Axis units, grey housings, fixed focal length. These were the newer Pelco Spectra series, matte black, with the slight convex bulge of a varifocal lens that could track a moving subject across three consecutive frames without operator input.

Kain had counted fourteen of them between Bourke and Parliament.

He boarded at the rear doors, tapped his Myki against the reader, and moved to the standing section without looking at the seats. The tram pulled away from the stop. The morning light came through the windows at a low angle, the kind of flat winter light that made every surface look provisional, and the carriage smelled of wet wool and the particular chemical sweetness of hand sanitiser applied over coffee breath.

Forty-three passengers. He had counted them at the stop.

The target was not among them.

\*\*\*

Collins Street. The tram slowed into the stop with the hydraulic sigh of the braking system, and the doors opened onto the grey stone canyon of the financial district. Eleven passengers disembarked. Seven boarded. The Pelco unit above the Collins Street shelter rotated three degrees on its mount — a micro-adjustment, the kind the system made when it was tracking a face rather than scanning a crowd — and Kain shifted his weight to his left foot, putting the standing pole between himself and the camera's primary axis.

Not evasion. Evasion triggered a flag. This was positioning — the difference between a man who knew where the cameras were and a man who was hiding from them.

The doors closed.

The tram moved.

He ran the morning's data. The café on Punt Road had produced a contact at 9:15 AM on three consecutive Tuesdays. The contact was male, mid-thirties, presenting in civilian clothing — no operational tells in the clothing itself, but the seating choice was consistent: the table nearest the rear exit, back to the wall, sightline to the street. The contact arrived before the target. The contact left after the target. The contact never paid — the target settled both bills, which was either a social convention or a financial control mechanism, and Kain had spent two days deciding it was the latter.

The target's name had been in the conference room for fourteen seconds.

He had read it. He had cross-referenced it against the personnel files. He had been three steps into the QSA internal registry when the Bleed had taken the conference room and given him back the Punt Road footpath, and when the Punt Road footpath had resolved back into the conference room, the name was gone from the screen and from his working memory simultaneously, which was not how Bleeds worked — Bleeds took the environment, not the cognition — which meant the erasure was not a Bleed artefact.

It meant something else had cleared the screen.

He had not reported this to Emily.

\*\*\*

Parliament Station. The tram surfaced from the underground section into the grey morning, and the light changed quality — harder, more direct — and the carriage shifted as passengers redistributed their weight in response to the gradient. Kain moved with the shift, keeping his position relative to the rear doors, and the Pelco unit at the Parliament stop tracked the tram's approach with the slow, deliberate rotation of a system that was not looking for a specific face but was logging all of them.

Fourteen cameras between Bourke and Parliament.

He had not touched the target at any of the previous surveillance points. He had not approached. He had not made contact. He had maintained the prescribed distance — twelve metres minimum, the threshold below which the QSA's behavioural analysis software began assigning intent scores to proximity — and he had documented the contact pattern and submitted the documentation to Emily, who had read it and said: \*"Continue observation. Do not engage."\*

That instruction was now forty-eight hours old.

The tram crossed the intersection at Spring Street. The State Library's stone facade passed on the left, and then the residential grid of East Melbourne began — narrower streets, older buildings, the particular density of a neighbourhood that had been expensive for long enough that it had stopped trying to look expensive. The surveillance array thinned here. VicTrack's older Axis units replaced the Pelco series at the Lansdowne Street stop, and the thermal imaging capability dropped out entirely.

Kain noted the gap.

He noted it the way a man notes a loose board in a floor he crosses every day — not with surprise, not with calculation, but with the automatic registration of a structural fact that might become relevant.

\*\*\*

Nicholson Street. The tram stopped.

The target boarded at the front doors.

The recognition was not a shock. It was not a revelation. It was the specific cognitive experience of a pattern completing — the way a word surfaces from the edge of recall, not arriving but simply \*present\*, as though it had always been there and the mind had only just stopped blocking it. The face was the face from the intake photograph. The face was the face from the QSA file. The face was the face that had been in the conference room for fourteen seconds before the screen cleared.

The face was his own.

Not similar. Not a resemblance that required interpretation. The bone structure was identical — the particular geometry of the orbital ridge, the jaw's angle, the way the hairline receded at the left temple two centimetres further than the right. The man was thirty-five years old and six feet tall and the rest of the physical data arrived in fragments, because the rest of the physical data was wrong.

The gait was wrong.

Not wrong in the way of injury — no lateral compensation, no favoured side. Wrong in the way of a system that had lost its calibration. The man moved through the boarding passengers with the specific, over-deliberate placement of someone who could not fully trust the information his feet were sending upward. Each step was a decision rather than a reflex. The left hand found the overhead rail before the body needed it, two beats early, the grip closing with a force that whitened the knuckles — not for balance, but for the anticipatory certainty that balance was about to fail.

The tram lurched forward from the stop.

The man's right hand shot out and caught the seatback beside him. The movement was fast and graceless, the movement of a reflex that had been trained into a body that no longer responded to training at the speed training required. He held the seatback for three seconds after the tram had stabilised. Then he released it, and the release was slow, the fingers uncurling one at a time, as though the hand required individual instructions.

He sat in the third seat from the rear. Left side. Window.

The sitting was not a single motion. It was a sequence — the left hand to the seatback, the controlled lowering, the right hand braced against the armrest, and then the final drop of the last few centimetres that the body could not manage with precision, the small, involuntary impact of a man whose proprioceptive system was no longer reporting the distance between himself and the surface below him accurately. He absorbed the impact without expression. The expression of someone who had been absorbing it for long enough that it no longer registered as an event.

Kain did not move.

The Pelco unit at the Nicholson Street stop had logged the boarding. The system had a face. The system was now running its cross-reference against the QSA registry, and the cross-reference would return a result in approximately ninety seconds, and the result would flag the proximity of two registered subjects — one active field operative, one flagged surveillance target — within the same enclosed transit environment, and the flag would go to Emily's terminal before Kain could reach the next stop.

Ninety seconds.

He counted them.

\*\*\*

The tram moved through the Fitzroy grid. Brunswick Street approaching. The morning traffic thickened at the intersection, and the tram slowed, and the carriage swayed with the deceleration, and the man in the third seat from the rear did not look at the window.

He looked at his hands.

The right hand was on his knee, palm down, and the tremor in it was not large — not the gross motor failure of advanced neurological collapse, but the fine, persistent oscillation of a system fighting to maintain stillness against a signal it could no longer suppress. The fingers moved in a rhythm that had nothing to do with the tram's movement. When the tram swayed, the hand swayed with it. When the tram steadied, the hand did not. The tremor continued at its own frequency, independent of the external environment, the output of an internal process that had stopped responding to the body's attempts at regulation.

The left hand held the folded piece of paper.

The paper was the kind that came from a printer rather than a notebook, folded and unfolded enough times that the creases had gone soft.
